Shirkah — Partnership
beginner
8 min
The Definition and Legal Proof of Partnership
Introduction to the book of partnership, its definition, proofs, and two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of shirkah (partnership) and identify the three sources of its legality from the text.

Core Idea
Partnership is rooted in Quran, Sunnah, and continuous practice; its linguistic meaning is the mixture of shares.

This passage opens the Book of Partnership (Kitab al-Shirkah) in the Hanafi madhhab. Before examining the two types, the author establishes the fundamental definition and the legal proofs that make partnership a valid and blessed arrangement. Understanding these proofs is essential because they determine the scope and applicability of partnership rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic definition of shirkah
Read the author's explanation: 'shirkah' linguistically means the mixture of two shares such that they become indistinguishable. The contract is named after this result.
Note that the contract is called shirkah because it causes the mixture.
2
Locate the three proofs of legality
Find the Quranic verse (4:12: 'then they are partners in the third'), the hadiths (the Prophet's partnership with al-Sa'ib and the divine hadith), and the continuous practice (ta'amul) without disapproval.
The divine hadith 'I am the third of the two partners' is a strong encouragement.
3
Understand the placement after the chapter on mafqud
The author explains two reasons: (1) property of one is a trust in the other's hand, (2) joint ownership may arise in missing person's property. This shows connection to other trust-related topics.
This placement is unique to Hanafi fiqh arrangement.
4
Recognize the two-type division
The passage states clearly that partnership is of two types: shirkat al-milk (ownership partnership) and shirkat al-'aqd (contract partnership). This is the foundational classification.
Later lessons will elaborate on each.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشركة
هي ضربان : شركة ملك (٣) وشركة عقد (٤).
(١) قوله : كتاب الشركة ؛ ذكر ابن الهمام والزيلعي وغيرهما : إنَّ الفقهاء إنما أوردوا بحث الشركة عقيب بحث المفقود بوجهين : ١. كون مال أحدهما أمانة في يد الآخر كما أنَّ مال المفقود أمانة في يد الحاضر. ٢. وكون الاشتراك قد يتحقق في مال المفقود، كما لو مات مورثه وله وارث به آخر، وهذه مناسبة خاصة، والأولى عامة فيهما وفي الآبق واللقيط واللقطة. والشركة : - بكسر الشين المعجمة وسكون الراء المهملة – على ما هو المشهور، ويجوز - فتح الشين مع كسر الراء ومع سكونها - لغةً : عبارة عن خليط النَّصيبين بحيث لا يتميز أحدهما عن الآخر، سمّي بها العقد المفيد لها ؛ لكونه سبباً لها، وشرعيتها : ١. بالكتاب ؛ فقد قال الله في آية المواريث : فَهُمْ شُرَكَاهُ فِي الثلث (٣). ٢. وبالسنة ؛ كحديث السائب : كان رسول الله شريكي في الجاهلية، أخرجه ابن ماجة، وأبو داود، والحاكم. وفي «سنن أبي داود»، و«مستدرك الحاكم» مرفوعاً : قال الله : «أنا ثالث الشريكين ما لم يَخُنْ أحدهما صاحبه، فإذا خان خرجت». ٣. وبالتعامل من زمان رسول الله وأصحابه ومجتهدي أمته إلى عصرنا هذا من غير نكير.
[٢] قوله : شركة ملك ؛ فسَّرَ بعضُهم الملك هاهنا بمطلق الاختصاص، وأدرج فيه (١) في «فتح القدير»(٥ : ٣٧٦). (۲) في تبيين الحقائق»(۳: ۳۱۲ - ۳۱۶). (۳) النساء : من الآية ١٢ . (٤) في سنن ابن ماجة (۲) : (٦٦٨) ، و (سنن أبي داود»(٤ : ٢٦٠)، وغيرها. (٥) في سنن أبي داود (۳) : (٢٥٦) ، و(المستدرك»(٢ : ٦٠)، وغيرها.
وهي أن يملك اثنان عَيْناً، وكلُّ كأجنبي في مال صاحبه. وشركة عقد :
[1] قوله : أن يملك ؛ بأي سبب من أسباب الملك اختيارياً كان كالبيع والهبة ، أو جبرياً كالإرث ، ومن الأول ما لو ملكا عيناً بهبة أو استيلاء على مال حربي، أو خلط مالهما بحيث لا يتميز ، أو قبلا وصيّة عين لهما ، ومن الثاني : ما لو اختلط مالهما بلا صنع من أحدهما. كذا في «البحر»). [٢ قوله : عيناً ؛ هو مقابل للدين، وهو احتراز عن الشركة في الدين، بناءً على أنَّ الدين ليس بمملوك. قال في «الفتح»(۲) : بعضُهم ذكر من شركة الأملاك : الشركة في الدين، فقيل: مجازاً ؛ لأنَّ الدين وصف شرعي لا يملك، وقد يقال : بل يملك شرعاً ؛ ولهذا جازَ هبتُهُ تمن عليه، وقد يقال : إن الهبة مجاز عن الإسقاط ؛ ولذا لم تجز من غير من عليه، والحق ما ذكروا من ملكه ؛ ولذا الملك عنه من العين على الإشراك حتـى لـو دفـع المديـون لأحدهما فللآخر الرجوع بنصف ما أخذ. [۳] قوله : وكلّ ؛ أي كلُّ واحدٍ من شركاء الملكِ كأجنبي في مال صاحبه ؛ لعدم تضمنها الوكالة، ويتفرّعُ عليه أنه يصح لأحد الشريكين بيع حصته شائعاً ، ولو من غير شريكه بلا إذنه إذا لم يضره. وأما إذا أضر بيعه شريكه فلا يجوز له بيعه من غير شريكه إلا بإذنه، كما في البناء والثمر والزرع المشترك ؛ فإنه يتضرر الشريك هاهنا بالقطع والهدم، وتفصيله في «الفصل الثلاثين» من «الفصول العمادية، وكذا لا يجوزُ البيع من الأجنبي بدون إذن الشريك في صورة الخلط، والاختلاط مطلقاً.
Book of Partnership. It is of two types: partnership in ownership and partnership by contract. (1) His statement: 'Book of Partnership'; Ibn al-Humam, al-Zayla'i, and others mentioned that the jurists placed the discussion of partnership after the discussion of the missing person for two reasons: 1. The property of one is a trust in the hand of the other, just as the property of the missing person is a trust in the hand of the present person. 2. And because joint ownership may occur in the property of the missing person, as if his ancestor dies and he has another heir with him. This is a specific suitability, and the first is general regarding both (partnership and missing person) and regarding the runaway slave, foundling, and lost item. Partnership – with a kasra on the shīn and sukun on the rā’ – according to what is well-known, and it is permissible (to pronounce) with a fatha on the shīn and kasra on the rā’ and with sukun on it – linguistically: an expression for the mixture of two shares such that one is not distinguishable from the other. The contract that leads to it is named after it because it is a cause for it. Its legality: 1. By the Book: Allah said in the verse of inheritance: 'Then they are partners in the third.' 2. By the Sunnah: like the hadith of al-Sa'ib: 'The Messenger of Allah was my partner in the pre-Islamic period,' narrated by Ibn Majah, Abu Dawud, and al-Hakim. And in Sunan Abi Dawud and Mustadrak al-Hakim as a marfu' hadith: Allah said: 'I am the third of the two partners as long as one of them does not betray his companion; when he betrays, I leave.' 3. By continuous practice from the time of the Messenger of Allah, his Companions, and the mujtahids of his community until our time, without any disapproval. [2] His statement: 'partnership in ownership'; some have interpreted 'ownership' here as absolute right of disposal and included in it... And it is that two people own a specific thing, and each is like a stranger regarding the property of the other. Partnership by contract: [1] His statement: 'that two own' – by any cause of ownership, whether voluntary like sale and gift, or compulsory like inheritance. From the first: if they own a specific thing by gift or seizure of enemy property, or mixing their property such that it is indistinguishable, or they accept a bequest of a specific thing for both. From the second: if their property mixes without any action from either of them. Thus in al-Bahr. [2] His statement: 'a specific thing' – it is opposed to debt, and it is a precaution regarding partnership in debt, based on the fact that debt is not owned. He said in al-Fath: Some mentioned among partnership in ownership: partnership in debt, so it was said metaphorically because debt is a legal description, not owned. And it may be said: rather it is owned legally; therefore gifting it is permitted as a free gift. And it may be said: gifting is a metaphor for dropping the claim; therefore it is not permitted from anyone other than the debtor. The correct view is what they mentioned of its being owned; therefore ownership of it is like ownership of a specific thing regarding joint ownership, such that if the debtor pays one of them, the other may claim half of what was taken. [3] His statement: 'and each' – that is, each one of the partners in ownership is like a stranger regarding the property of his partner, because it does not include agency. Derived from this is that it is valid for one partner to sell his undivided share, even to a non-partner without his permission if it does not harm him. But if his sale harms his partner, then it is not permissible for him to sell to a non-partner except with his permission, as in jointly owned buildings, trees, and crops, because the partner is harmed here by cutting and demolition. Its details are in the thirtieth chapter of Fusul al-'Imadi. Similarly, sale to an outsider without the partner's permission is not allowed in the case of mixing, and mixing absolutely.
Source pages p. 545

Commentary
This passage begins the Book of Partnership (Kitab al-Shirkah) in the Hanafi madhhab. The author opens by stating that partnership is of two types: partnership in ownership (shirkat al-milk) and partnership by contract (shirkat al-'aqd). The first type refers to joint ownership of a specific asset where each owner is considered a stranger with respect to the other's share, meaning no agency is implied. The second type is a contractual partnership that will be explained later.

Before defining the types, the commentator explains why the topic of partnership is placed after the chapter on the missing person (mafqud). Two reasons are given: first, in both cases one party's property is a trust in the hands of another; second, joint ownership may arise in the property of a missing person. This connection is also relevant to other topics like the runaway slave, foundling, and lost item.

Linguistically, 'shirkah' means the mixture of two shares such that they become indistinguishable. The contract that creates such a mixture is named after the resulting state because it is the cause.

The legality of partnership is established from three sources: (1) The Quran, in the verse of inheritance (4:12) where Allah says 'they are partners in the third'; (2) The Sunnah, including the hadith that the Prophet ﷺ was a partner of al-Sa'ib in the pre-Islamic period, and the divine hadith 'I am the third of the two partners as long as they do not betray each other'; (3) Continuous practice (ta'amul) from the Prophet's time to the present without any disapproval (inkar).

The first type, shirkat al-milk, is then explained in detail. It occurs when two people own a specific thing (ayn) through any cause of ownership, whether voluntary (like sale, gift, or mixing their property) or compulsory (like inheritance or accidental mixing). 'Ayn' is specified to exclude debt, as debt is not considered owned property in the same way. There is some discussion among scholars about whether partnership in debt is possible; the preferred view is that debt can be owned, so if a debtor pays one partner, the other can claim half.

An important consequence of shirkat al-milk is that each partner is like a stranger (ajnabi) regarding the other's share because no agency (wakala) is implied. Therefore, a partner may sell his undivided share to anyone, including a non-partner, without the other partner's permission, as long as it does not cause harm. However, if the sale would cause harm (e.g., selling a share of a jointly owned building, tree, or crop that requires demolition or cutting), then the permission of the other partner is required. This is detailed in the thirtieth chapter of Fusul al-'Imadi. The same restriction applies in cases of thorough mixing (khalṭ) where separation is impossible.

Key Terms
شركة (shirkah): Partnership: the mixture of two shares such that they are indistinguishable; also the contract that creates this state.
شركة ملك (shirkat al-milk): Partnership in ownership: joint ownership of a specific asset by two or more persons without any agency agreement.
شركة عقد (shirkat al-'aqd): Partnership by contract: a partnership formed by mutual agreement where each partner is an agent for the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence from the Quran for the legality of partnership according to this passage?
A) 'Then they are partners in the third' (4:12)
B) 'Cooperate in righteousness and piety' (5:2)
C) 'And do not devour your property among yourselves falsely' (2:188)
D) 'So if they repent and establish prayer and give zakah, then they are your brothers in religion' (9:11)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi madhhab recognizes two types of partnership: ownership and contract, with legal proofs from Quran, Sunnah, and continuous practice.

Shirkah — Partnership
intermediate
10 min
Partnership in Ownership (Shirkat al-Milk): Concept and Rulings
Detailed explanation of shirkat al-milk: definition, conditions, and practical rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of shirkat al-milk, state its key condition (ownership of ayn), and apply the rule that each partner is a stranger regarding the other's share.

Core Idea
In shirkat al-milk, each partner is a stranger to the other's share — no agency, no permission needed for sale unless harm occurs.

After establishing the general validity of partnership, the text delves into the first type: partnership in ownership (shirkat al-milk). This type arises naturally through inheritance or voluntary acts like purchase and mixing. Its key feature is the absence of agency, which has important practical consequences for how partners may transact with their shares.

Guided Path
1
Define shirkat al-milk and its key condition
Read that shirkat al-milk occurs when two people own a specific thing (ayn). 'Ayn' is opposed to debt, so partnership in debt is controversial. Understand why the Hanafis prefer that debt can be owned.
The condition of being 'ayn' excludes partnership in debts according to some; the Hanafi view allows it with some discussion.
2
List the causes of ownership
The text divides causes into voluntary (sale, gift, mixing, acceptance of bequest) and compulsory (inheritance, accidental mixing). Identify each from the passage.
Voluntary mixing: if two people deliberately mix their property so that it cannot be distinguished, they become partners in ownership.
3
Understand the rule: each partner is a stranger
The author says 'each is like a stranger regarding the property of his partner' because shirkat al-milk does not include agency. This means no one has authority over the other's share by default.
This is the opposite of shirkat al-'aqd where mutual agency exists.
4
Apply the rule to sale of shares
Consequences: a partner may sell his undivided share to a third party without the other partner's permission, unless the sale causes harm (e.g., selling a share of a co-owned building that requires demolition). In such cases, permission is needed.
The text references the thirtieth chapter of Fusul al-'Imadi for details.

كتاب الشركة

Book of Partnership. It is of two types: partnership in ownership and partnership by contract. (1) His statement: 'Book of Partnership'; Ibn al-Humam, al-Zayla'i, and others mentioned that the jurists placed the discussion of partnership after the discussion of the missing person for two reasons: 1. The property of one is a trust in the hand of the other, just as the property of the missing person is a trust in the hand of the present person. 2. And because joint ownership may occur in the property of the missing person, as if his ancestor dies and he has another heir with him. This is a specific suitability, and the first is general regarding both (partnership and missing person) and regarding the runaway slave, foundling, and lost item. Partnership – with a kasra on the shīn and sukun on the rā’ – according to what is well-known, and it is permissible (to pronounce) with a fatha on the shīn and kasra on the rā’ and with sukun on it – linguistically: an expression for the mixture of two shares such that one is not distinguishable from the other. The contract that leads to it is named after it because it is a cause for it. Its legality: 1. By the Book: Allah said in the verse of inheritance: 'Then they are partners in the third.' 2. By the Sunnah: like the hadith of al-Sa'ib: 'The Messenger of Allah was my partner in the pre-Islamic period,' narrated by Ibn Majah, Abu Dawud, and al-Hakim. And in Sunan Abi Dawud and Mustadrak al-Hakim as a marfu' hadith: Allah said: 'I am the third of the two partners as long as one of them does not betray his companion; when he betrays, I leave.' 3. By continuous practice from the time of the Messenger of Allah, his Companions, and the mujtahids of his community until our time, without any disapproval. [2] His statement: 'partnership in ownership'; some have interpreted 'ownership' here as absolute right of disposal and included in it... And it is that two people own a specific thing, and each is like a stranger regarding the property of the other. Partnership by contract: [1] His statement: 'that two own' – by any cause of ownership, whether voluntary like sale and gift, or compulsory like inheritance. From the first: if they own a specific thing by gift or seizure of enemy property, or mixing their property such that it is indistinguishable, or they accept a bequest of a specific thing for both. From the second: if their property mixes without any action from either of them. Thus in al-Bahr. [2] His statement: 'a specific thing' – it is opposed to debt, and it is a precaution regarding partnership in debt, based on the fact that debt is not owned. He said in al-Fath: Some mentioned among partnership in ownership: partnership in debt, so it was said metaphorically because debt is a legal description, not owned. And it may be said: rather it is owned legally; therefore gifting it is permitted as a free gift. And it may be said: gifting is a metaphor for dropping the claim; therefore it is not permitted from anyone other than the debtor. The correct view is what they mentioned of its being owned; therefore ownership of it is like ownership of a specific thing regarding joint ownership, such that if the debtor pays one of them, the other may claim half of what was taken. [3] His statement: 'and each' – that is, each one of the partners in ownership is like a stranger regarding the property of his partner, because it does not include agency. Derived from this is that it is valid for one partner to sell his undivided share, even to a non-partner without his permission if it does not harm him. But if his sale harms his partner, then it is not permissible for him to sell to a non-partner except with his permission, as in jointly owned buildings, trees, and crops, because the partner is harmed here by cutting and demolition. Its details are in the thirtieth chapter of Fusul al-'Imadi. Similarly, sale to an outsider without the partner's permission is not allowed in the case of mixing, and mixing absolutely.

Commentary
This passage begins the Book of Partnership (Kitab al-Shirkah) in the Hanafi madhhab. The author opens by stating that partnership is of two types: partnership in ownership (shirkat al-milk) and partnership by contract (shirkat al-'aqd). The first type refers to joint ownership of a specific asset where each owner is considered a stranger with respect to the other's share, meaning no agency is implied. The second type is a contractual partnership that will be explained later.

Before defining the types, the commentator explains why the topic of partnership is placed after the chapter on the missing person (mafqud). Two reasons are given: first, in both cases one party's property is a trust in the hands of another; second, joint ownership may arise in the property of a missing person. This connection is also relevant to other topics like the runaway slave, foundling, and lost item.

Linguistically, 'shirkah' means the mixture of two shares such that they become indistinguishable. The contract that creates such a mixture is named after the resulting state because it is the cause.

The legality of partnership is established from three sources: (1) The Quran, in the verse of inheritance (4:12) where Allah says 'they are partners in the third'; (2) The Sunnah, including the hadith that the Prophet ﷺ was a partner of al-Sa'ib in the pre-Islamic period, and the divine hadith 'I am the third of the two partners as long as they do not betray each other'; (3) Continuous practice (ta'amul) from the Prophet's time to the present without any disapproval (inkar).

The first type, shirkat al-milk, is then explained in detail. It occurs when two people own a specific thing (ayn) through any cause of ownership, whether voluntary (like sale, gift, or mixing their property) or compulsory (like inheritance or accidental mixing). 'Ayn' is specified to exclude debt, as debt is not considered owned property in the same way. There is some discussion among scholars about whether partnership in debt is possible; the preferred view is that debt can be owned, so if a debtor pays one partner, the other can claim half.

An important consequence of shirkat al-milk is that each partner is like a stranger (ajnabi) regarding the other's share because no agency (wakala) is implied. Therefore, a partner may sell his undivided share to anyone, including a non-partner, without the other partner's permission, as long as it does not cause harm. However, if the sale would cause harm (e.g., selling a share of a jointly owned building, tree, or crop that requires demolition or cutting), then the permission of the other partner is required. This is detailed in the thirtieth chapter of Fusul al-'Imadi. The same restriction applies in cases of thorough mixing (khalṭ) where separation is impossible.

Key Terms
شركة ملك (shirkat al-milk): Partnership in ownership: joint ownership of a specific asset by two or more persons without any agency agreement.
عين (ayn): Specific property or asset, as opposed to debt or benefit.
خلط (khalṭ): Mixing of property such that shares become indistinguishable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition can a partner in shirkat al-milk sell his share without the other partner's permission?
A) Always, because each owns his share absolutely.
B) Only if the other partner is given the right of first refusal.
C) Always, as long as the sale does not harm the other partner.
D) Only if the sale is to a relative.

Key Takeaway
In shirkat al-milk, each partner is independent and can sell his share unless harm results; no agency exists.

Joint Ownership (Shirkat al-Milk): Definition and Core Rulings
Defines joint ownership and its legal consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define shirkat al-milk, distinguish it from contractual partnership, and state when a co-owner may sell his share without the other's permission.

Core Idea
In joint ownership, each co-owner is a stranger to the other's share — no agency, and selling is restricted when it causes harm.

Joint ownership (shirkat al-milk) arises when two or more persons own a tangible asset together, whether by choice (purchase, gift) or by force (inheritance, accidental mixing). This lesson unpacks the Hanafi definition, explains the key distinction from contractual partnership, and clarifies the crucial limitation on selling one's share: the other co-owner's interest must not be harmed. We will also touch on the special case of mixed property and the debate over partnership in debt.

Guided Path
1
Deconstruct the definition
Identify the three essential components in the text: (1) possession of a tangible asset ('ayn), (2) two or more owners, (3) each is like a stranger regarding the other's property. The third point means no automatic agency.
The phrase 'like a stranger' is key: you cannot act for your co-owner without permission.
2
Identify the causes of joint ownership
The commentary lists optional causes (sale, gift, seizure of enemy property) and compulsory causes (inheritance, mixing without the act of either party). Understand that mixing (khalṭ) is a cause only when it happens without deliberation.
For example, two people's grains mix accidentally in a shared granary – they become joint owners.
3
Apply the rule on selling shares
The text states that a co-owner may sell his undivided share to a third party without the other's permission ONLY if the sale does not harm the other co-owner. If harm would occur (e.g., forced demolition of a shared building), permission is required.
The harm is judged by the likely effect, not the co-owner's mere dislike.
4
Recognize the mixing exception
In cases of completely mixed property (where shares are indistinguishable), sale to a stranger is absolutely prohibited without the co-owner's consent. This is stricter than ordinary joint ownership.
Reason: with mixed property, partition is impossible without mutual agreement.

والشجر والزرع المشترك ؛ فإنه يتضرر الشريك هاهنا بالقطع والهدم، وتفصيله في «الفصل الثلاثين» من «الفصول العمادية، وكذا لا يجوزُ البيع من الأجنبي بدون إذن الشريك في صورة الخلط، والاختلاط مطلقاً.
And it is that two persons possess a specific asset, and each is like a stranger regarding the property of the other. And contractual partnership is that two persons possess a specific asset, and each is like a stranger regarding the property of the other. 2. And contractual partnership: Partnership of multiple persons in a thing for preservation, like a garment that the wind blew into their house, so both are partners in its preservation, and the right belongs to both, not to one of them. [1] His statement: 'that they possess' – by any cause of ownership, whether optional like sale or gift, or compulsory like inheritance. From the first: if they possess a specific asset by gift or seizure of enemy property, or mixing their property such that it is indistinguishable, or they accept a bequest of a specific asset for both. From the second: if their property mixes without the act of one of them. Thus in 'al-Bahr'. [2] His statement: 'a specific asset' – it is the opposite of debt, and it is a precaution against partnership in debt, based on the fact that debt is not owned. He said in 'al-Fath': Some mentioned that among joint ownerships is partnership in debt. It was said: metaphorically, because debt is a legal description that is not owned. And it may be said: rather it is owned legally; for this reason its gift is valid as a favor. And it may be said: the gift is a metaphor for dropping the claim; therefore it is not valid from other than the debtor. The truth is what they mentioned of its ownership; therefore ownership [؟] from the specific asset upon partnership, so if the debtor pays one of them, the other has the right to reclaim half of what was taken. [3] His statement: 'and each' – i.e., each one of the joint owners is like a stranger regarding the property of the other, because it does not entail agency. And it branches from this that it is valid for one of the two partners to sell his undivided share, even from other than his partner without his permission, if it does not harm him. But if his sale harms his partner, then it is not permissible for him to sell it to other than his partner except with his permission, as in the case of common building, trees, and crops; for the partner is harmed here by cutting and demolition. Its details are in the thirtieth chapter of 'al-Fusul al-Imadiyya'. Likewise, sale to a stranger without the partner's permission is not allowed in the case of mixing, and mixing absolutely.
Source pages p. 546

Commentary
This passage defines joint ownership (shirkat al-milk) where two or more persons share ownership of a specific asset ('ayn). It clarifies that in such ownership, each co-owner is considered a stranger with respect to the other's share, meaning they do not have agency to act on each other's behalf. The commentary explains the causes of joint ownership—whether voluntary (e.g., purchase, gift) or involuntary (e.g., inheritance, mixing without intent). It also addresses the distinction between 'ayn (tangible asset) and dayn (debt), noting that debt is not considered owned in the same way, though some jurists debate the permissibility of gifting debt. The practical ruling derived is that a co-owner may sell his undivided share to a third party without permission only if it causes no harm to the other co-owner. If harm arises, such as in shared buildings or trees where demolition/cutting would damage the other's interest, permission is required. Similarly, in cases of mixed indistinguishable property, sale to a stranger is not allowed without the other's consent.

Key Terms
شركة ملك (shirkat al-milk): Joint ownership of a specific asset arising from causes like inheritance, purchase, mixing, etc., where each owner holds a distinct share but the asset is undivided.
عين ('ayn): A specific tangible asset, as opposed to a debt (dayn) which is a legal obligation.
خلط (khalṭ): Mixing of properties so that they become indistinguishable, potentially creating joint ownership even without intent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition may one joint owner sell his share to a third party without the other's permission?
A) Always, regardless of circumstances
B) Only if the sale causes harm to the other partner
C) Only if the sale does not cause harm to the other partner
D) Only if the property is mixed indistinguishably

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, joint ownership of a tangible asset gives each co-owner the right to sell his share, but this right is restricted to prevent harm to the other co-owners, and is completely blocked when the property is mixed indistinguishably.

Pillars and Conditions of Contractual Partnership
Defines the pillar and condition of contractual partnership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the pillar (rukn) of shirkat al-'aqd and state the condition that invalidates it.

Core Idea
Stipulating a fixed profit for one partner cuts the partnership—sharing must be proportional.

In Hanafi commercial law, a valid contractual partnership rests on clear offer and acceptance. But even if the form is correct, a hidden stipulation can destroy the contract. This lesson examines the essential element and the critical condition that safeguards the partnership's nature.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Pillar
In the text, find the statement: 'وركتها الإيجاب والقبول'. This is the rukn of shirkat al-'aqd.
The rukn is the constitutive element without which the contract does not exist.
2
Identify the Condition
The text says: 'وشرطها عدمُ ما يقطعها' and gives an example: stipulating fixed dirhams of profit for one partner.
This condition is a shart that must be absent for the contract to be valid.
3
Understand Why It Cuts
Because profit is uncertain: after the fixed amount, there may be nothing left to share. This violates the essential profit-sharing nature of partnership.
4
Note the Consequence
If such a condition is present, the partnership is invalid (fāsid) in Hanafi law.
The contract is not merely voidable but void ab initio.

Source Text

وهي أربعة أوجه : مفاوضة : وهي شركة متساويين مالاً وتصرفاً

[٤] قوله : مالاً ؛ أي يكون مالهما متساوياً، وكذا يكونان متساويين تصرفاً، حتى لو ملك أحدهما تصرُّفاً لا يملكه الآخر، فإنَّ [المساواة] بأن كان أحدهما صبياً أو ذميّاً أو

Book of Partnership: Its Types and Rulings. Its pillar is offer and acceptance, and its condition is the absence of anything that cuts it, like stipulating named dirhams of profit for one of them. And it has four types: It has four types: Mufawada: a partnership of equals in wealth and action. Its pillar is offer and acceptance, and its condition is the absence of anything that cuts it, like stipulating named dirhams of profit for one of them; for this cuts the partnership because it is possible that after these named dirhams there remains no profit for them to share. (It has four types: 1. Mufawada: a partnership of equals in wealth, action, and debt.) And the difference: If the partnership is from the beginning, e.g., they buy wheat or inherit it, then every grain is common between them, so each sells his own share as an undivided part, and it is permissible from the partner and the stranger. As opposed to when it is by mixing; because every grain is owned in its entirety by one of them, the other has no share in it. So if he sells to other than the partner, he cannot deliver except the partner's share, so it depends on his permission. Unlike selling to the partner, because there is ability to deliver and receive. Thus in 'al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriya'. [1] His statement: offer and acceptance – such that one says: I partner with you in such-and-such, and the other says: I accept. It is conditioned that the transaction contracted upon in the partnership contract be susceptible to agency, so that what is gained by the transaction becomes common between them, thus its desired ruling is realized. Therefore, partnership in collecting firewood, grass, hunting, and the like is not allowed. Thus in 'al-`Inaya'. [2] His statement: like stipulating named dirhams – i.e., specified, such that one says: I partner with you on the condition that I specifically get, for example, one hundred dirhams from the profit, and the rest is shared. [3] His statement: mufawada – derived from fawz, from which fāḍ al-mā' (water flowed) when it spreads, or from tafwīḍ meaning equality. [4] His statement: wealth – i.e., their wealth is equal, and likewise they are equal in action, so if one of them possesses an action that the other does not, then equality fails; if one is a child, a dhimmi, or a slave, mufawada is not valid.
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This passage introduces contractual partnership (shirkat al-'aqd), which is a contract based on offer and acceptance (ijab wa qubul). Its key condition is that nothing should cut the partnership, such as stipulating a fixed amount of profit for one partner, as this would negate the sharing of profit essential to partnership. The text then states that there are four types, beginning with mufawada (full partnership). Mufawada requires equality between partners in three aspects: wealth (capital), action (authority to transact), and debt (liability). The commentary explains that the subject of the contract must be susceptible to agency, so partnerships in activities like collecting wood or hunting are invalid. It also clarifies the difference between initial joint ownership (e.g., buying or inheriting together) and later mixing: in the former, each partner owns an undivided share of every grain, so they can sell their share to anyone; in the latter, each grain is owned entirely by one, so sale to a stranger requires the other's permission. Mufawada is invalid if one partner lacks full capacity (e.g., a child, dhimmi with limited rights, or slave).

Key Terms
شركة عقد (shirkat al-'aqd): Contractual partnership formed by offer and acceptance for the purpose of profit-sharing, where each partner is agent and surety for the other (within the scope of the contract).
ركن الشركة (rukn al-shirkah): The essential element of the partnership contract, namely offer and acceptance (ijab wa qubul).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following stipulations would invalidate a contractual partnership according to the text?
A) Equal profit sharing
B) Fixed profit amount for one partner
C) Loss sharing equally
D) Time limit of one year

Key Takeaway
The pillar of shirkat al-'aqd is offer and acceptance; stipulating a fixed profit for a partner cuts the essential profit-sharing and invalidates the contract.

Shirkah — Partnership

Mufawada: Full Partnership and Its Requirements
Introduces mufawada as the first type of contractual partnership and discusses its equality conditions and a distinction in ownership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three equalities required for a valid mufawada partnership and explain why initial joint ownership differs from mixing.

Core Idea
Mufawada requires partners to be equal in wealth, authority, and debt liability; any inequality breaks the partnership.

Mufawada is the most comprehensive form of partnership in Hanafi law. It demands strict equality, reflecting the idea that each partner is both agent and surety for the other. This lesson unpacks its conditions and clarifies a common confusion about ownership.

Guided Path
1
Define Mufawada
The text says: 'مفاوضة : وهي شركة متساويين مالاً وتصرفاً ودينا'. Understand the etymology from fawz (spreading) or tafwid (equality).
Mufawada is a unique Hanafi institution; other schools do not have an exact counterpart.
2
Understand the Three Equalities
Equality in capital (wealth), authority to transact (action), and liability for debts.
If any equality is lacking, the mufawada is invalid.
3
Recognize Invalid Cases
The text gives examples: if one partner is a child, a dhimmi, or a slave, equality of capacity fails, so mufawada is not valid.
Full legal capacity is required for both partners.
4
Distinguish Initial vs. Mixed Ownership
The commentary explains that if they buy or inherit together, each owns an undivided share of every grain; if they mix separately owned goods, each grain is owned entirely by one, so sale to a stranger requires the other's permission.
This affects how profits and losses are allocated.

Book of Partnership: Its Types and Rulings. Its pillar is offer and acceptance, and its condition is the absence of anything that cuts it, like stipulating named dirhams of profit for one of them. And it has four types: It has four types: Mufawada: a partnership of equals in wealth and action. Its pillar is offer and acceptance, and its condition is the absence of anything that cuts it, like stipulating named dirhams of profit for one of them; for this cuts the partnership because it is possible that after these named dirhams there remains no profit for them to share. (It has four types: 1. Mufawada: a partnership of equals in wealth, action, and debt.) And the difference: If the partnership is from the beginning, e.g., they buy wheat or inherit it, then every grain is common between them, so each sells his own share as an undivided part, and it is permissible from the partner and the stranger. As opposed to when it is by mixing; because every grain is owned in its entirety by one of them, the other has no share in it. So if he sells to other than the partner, he cannot deliver except the partner's share, so it depends on his permission. Unlike selling to the partner, because there is ability to deliver and receive. Thus in 'al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriya'. [1] His statement: offer and acceptance – such that one says: I partner with you in such-and-such, and the other says: I accept. It is conditioned that the transaction contracted upon in the partnership contract be susceptible to agency, so that what is gained by the transaction becomes common between them, thus its desired ruling is realized. Therefore, partnership in collecting firewood, grass, hunting, and the like is not allowed. Thus in 'al-`Inaya'. [2] His statement: like stipulating named dirhams – i.e., specified, such that one says: I partner with you on the condition that I specifically get, for example, one hundred dirhams from the profit, and the rest is shared. [3] His statement: mufawada – derived from fawz, from which fāḍ al-mā' (water flowed) when it spreads, or from tafwīḍ meaning equality. [4] His statement: wealth – i.e., their wealth is equal, and likewise they are equal in action, so if one of them possesses an action that the other does not, then equality fails; if one is a child, a dhimmi, or a slave, mufawada is not valid.
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This passage introduces contractual partnership (shirkat al-'aqd), which is a contract based on offer and acceptance (ijab wa qubul). Its key condition is that nothing should cut the partnership, such as stipulating a fixed amount of profit for one partner, as this would negate the sharing of profit essential to partnership. The text then states that there are four types, beginning with mufawada (full partnership). Mufawada requires equality between partners in three aspects: wealth (capital), action (authority to transact), and debt (liability). The commentary explains that the subject of the contract must be susceptible to agency, so partnerships in activities like collecting wood or hunting are invalid. It also clarifies the difference between initial joint ownership (e.g., buying or inheriting together) and later mixing: in the former, each partner owns an undivided share of every grain, so they can sell their share to anyone; in the latter, each grain is owned entirely by one, so sale to a stranger requires the other's permission. Mufawada is invalid if one partner lacks full capacity (e.g., a child, dhimmi with limited rights, or slave).

Key Terms
مفاوضة (mufāwaḍah): Full partnership where partners are equal in capital, authority to act, and liability for debts; each acts as agent and surety for the other.
شركة عقد (shirkat al-'aqd): Contractual partnership formed by offer and acceptance for the purpose of profit-sharing, where each partner is agent and surety for the other (within the scope of the contract).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following makes mufawada invalid?
A) One partner is a minor
B) Different capital amounts
C) One partner has no right to act for the partnership
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Mufawada requires strict equality in wealth, authority, and debt; any inequality or legal incapacity invalidates the partnership.

Condition of Religious Equality in Mufawada Partnership
Explains the condition of religious unity for valid Mufawada with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the requirement of religious unity for Mufawada partnership in the Hanafi school, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and apply the mufta bihi position to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Mufawada requires equality in religion; Abu Hanifa rejects Muslim-non-Muslim partnerships; Abu Yusuf permits them.

This lesson examines the condition of religious unity (ittihad al-milla) for the validity of a Mufawada (full) partnership. While the general requirement is that partners be equal in freedom, maturity, and religion, the Hanafi school witnessed an internal disagreement over whether a Muslim can enter into Mufawada with a non-Muslim. We will explore the proofs of both sides and identify the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions for Mufawada
From the text: 'It is not valid except between those united in freedom, sanity, and religion.' Recognize that religion is one of the three equality requirements.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
According to Abu Hanifa, a Mufawada partnership between a Muslim and a non-Muslim is invalid. He argues that 'all disbelief is one religion' (kufr kulluhu milla wahida), and the difference in permissible transactions (e.g., selling wine) breaks the required equality in disposal.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that a Mufawada between a Muslim and a non-Muslim is valid. His proof: both are equal in the capacity for agency (wakala) and suretyship (kafala), which are the basis of the partnership.
He considers the difference in disposal to be irrelevant.
4
Analyze the qiyas (analogical reasoning)
Formalize the argument: Asl = agency/suretyship (valid between Muslim and non-Muslim), Far = Mufawada, Illa = equal capacity for agency. Abu Hanifa's objection: the difference in legal capacity for certain transactions breaks the equality required for Mufawada.
This is a qiyas khafi (obscure analogy).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
The practical difference: if a Muslim and a non-Muslim form a Mufawada, according to Abu Hanifa it is fasid (invalid) and profits are not shared per partnership rules; according to Abu Yusuf it is sahih and profits are shared as per contract. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي أربعة أوجه : مفاوضة : وهي شركة متساويين مالاً وتصرفاً ودينا) ... (فلا تصح إلا بين متحدين حرية وحلماً وملة) ... (سواء كان أحدهما كتابياً والآخر مجوسياً ، فإِنَّ الكفر كله ملةٌ واحدة، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة له ، وعند أبي يوسف له تجوز بين المسلم والكافر) قوله : تجوز ... الخ ؛ له أنَّ الكافر والمسلم متساويان في الوكالة والكفالة، والاعتبار لزيادة تصرف يملكُهُ أحدهما دونَ الآخر.
And it is of four types: Mufawada: it is a partnership of two equals in capital, disposal, and debt... (It is not valid except between those united in freedom, sanity, and religion)... (Whether one is a Scriptuary and the other a Magian, for disbelief is all one religion; this is according to Abu Hanifa, and according to Abu Yusuf it is permissible between a Muslim and a disbeliever) His statement: 'it is permissible' etc.; for him, the disbeliever and Muslim are equal in agency and suretyship, and consideration is for the increase in disposal that one possesses over the other.
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya discusses the condition of religious unity for the validity of a Mufawada partnership (full partnership). The author states that Mufawada is not valid except between parties who are equal in freedom, legal capacity (hilm, i.e., maturity and sanity), and religion (milla). The commentator then adds an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Abu Hanifa, all disbelievers are considered one religion for this purpose, so partnership between a Muslim and a non-Muslim is invalid. He even cites the case of a Jew/Zoroastrian as permissible among non-Muslims. However, Abu Yusuf holds that a Mufawada partnership between a Muslim and a non-Muslim is permissible. Abu Yusuf's reasoning is that both are equal in the capacity for agency (wakala) and suretyship (kafala), and the validity of the partnership depends on the ability to perform such acts; since both can act as agents, religious difference does not prevent equality in disposal. The commentator clarifies that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa, as it is more cautious and aligns with the general principle that Mufawada requires complete equality in all aspects of disposal, including religious obligations that might affect legal transactions.

Key Terms
مفاوضة (mufāwaḍah): A full partnership where partners are equal in capital, disposal, and debt, with mutual agency and suretyship.
ملة (millah): Religion or religious community; here used to mean the same religion for validity of partnership.
حلم (ḥilm): Legal maturity and sanity; the condition that both partners be adults of sound mind.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, is a Mufawada partnership valid between a Muslim and a Christian?
A) Yes, because both are People of the Book.
B) No, because they are not united in religion.
C) Yes, because they are equal in agency.
D) No, because a Christian cannot own property.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a Mufawada partnership requires that the partners be of the same religion; the mufta bihi is that a Muslim cannot enter Mufawada with a non-Muslim.

Conditions for Valid Mufawada: Equality in Freedom, Maturity, and Religion
Explaining the conditions of equality for mufawada partnership and the intra-Hanafi dispute with Sahibayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions of equality required for a valid mufawada partnership and explain why a Muslim-non-Muslim mufawada is invalid according to the majority Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Mufawada requires equality in freedom, maturity, and religion; a Muslim and non-Muslim cannot form a valid mufawada due to fundamental differences in lawful disposition.

The mufawada partnership is a unique Hanafi contract built on mutual agency and surety. This lesson examines the conditions that make it valid, focusing on the often-misunderstood requirement of religious unity. We will explore an intra-Hanafi dispute that tests the boundaries of legal equality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions of equality
From the text, extract the three attributes in which partners must be united: freedom (both free), maturity (both adults), and religion (same milla). Understand that these are prerequisites for the mutual agency and surety inherent in mufawada.
Text: 'فلا تصح إلا بين متحدين حرية وحلماً وملة' – it is valid only between two united in freedom, maturity, and religion.
2
Understand why each condition is necessary
Freedom ensures full ownership and capacity to transact; maturity ensures legal majority; same religion ensures that both partners have the same scope of lawful transactions (tasarruf). A slave lacks independent disposition, a minor lacks capacity, and a non-Muslim can legally trade wine/pork while a Muslim cannot.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the religion condition
While Abu Hanifa (and Muhammad) hold that mufawada is not valid between a Muslim and a non-Muslim, Abu Yusuf disagrees and allows it. The text presents both views and their proofs.
Abu Yusuf's view: 'تجوز بين المسلم والكافر' – it is valid between Muslim and disbeliever.
4
Analyze the proofs of both sides
Abu Yusuf argues that both are equal in agency and surety. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad respond that true equality is lacking because a non-Muslim can buy wine/pork with partnership capital, while a Muslim cannot – creating an imbalance in disposition.
Dalil of the majority: 'فإن الذمي لو اشترى بالرأس المال خموراً أو خنازير صح ولو اشتراها مسلم لا يصح'.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: mufawada between a Muslim and a non-Muslim is invalid. Practically, such a partnership cannot be legally formed; if attempted, it must be dissolved.
The text also notes that Malik and Shafi'i reject mufawada entirely, further narrowing its application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(فلا تصح إلا بين متحدين" حرية وحلماً وملة) لا بُدَّ أن يكونا حرين بالغين، ملتهما واحدة، فلا تصح بين مسلم وكافر، وتجوز بين مسلمين بالغين، وبين كافرين، سواء كان أحدهما كتابيًا والآخرُ مجوسياً ، فإِنَّ الكفر كله ملةٌ واحدة، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة له ، وعند أبي يوسف له تجوز" بين المسلم والكافر
[٢]قوله : بين متحدين ... الخ ؛ فلا تصح بين حر وبين عبد ولو مكاتباً أو مأذوناً ؛ لعدم تساويهما تصرّفاً، فإنَّ المملوك لا يملك التَّصرُّفَ والكفالة إلا بإذن المولى، ولا تصح بين صبي وبين بالغ ؛ لأنَّ الصبي لا يملك تصرفاً إلا بإذن الولي بخلاف البالغ. ولا تصح بين المسلم وبين الكافر، وتجوز فيما بين الكفار، وفيما بين المسلمين كلهم، حتى تجوز فيما بين الحنفي والشافعي مع اختلافهما تصرفاً من وجه، فإنَّ التصرُّف في متروكِ التسمية عامداً جائز عند الشافعي لا عند الحنفي.
[٥] قوله : كله ملّة واحدة ؛ فإنَّ الكفّار وإن اختلفت نحلهم وتفرقت شعبهم قد جمعهم الكفر، ولهذا يجري التوارث فيما بينهم، كما أنَّ الإسلام ملة واحدة وإن افترقت المذاهب وتباينت المشارب.
[٦] قوله : تجوز ... الخ ؛ له أنَّ الكافر والمسلم متساويان في الوكالة والكفالة، والاعتبار لزيادة تصرف يملكُهُ أحدهما دونَ الآخر.
وَلَهُمَا : إنه لا تساوي بين المسلم والكافر تصرُّفاً ؛ فإنَّ الذمي لو اشترى برأس المال خموراً أو خنازير صح، ولو اشتراها مسلم لا يصح.
وَعِنْدَ مَالِكٍ وَالشَّافِعِيِّ لا تجوز المفاوضة أصلاً.
So it (mufawada) is valid only between two who are united in freedom, maturity, and religion. They must both be free adults of the same religion. Thus it is not valid between a Muslim and a disbeliever. It is valid between two adult Muslims and between two disbelievers, equally whether one is a scriptuary and the other a Zoroastrian, for all disbelief is one religion. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to Abu Yusuf, it is valid between a Muslim and a disbeliever. [Commentary: It is not valid between a free man and a slave, even a mukatab or a ma'dhun, because they are not equal in disposition; the slave does not possess disposition and surety except with the master's permission. Nor valid between a minor and an adult because the minor cannot transact except with permission of guardian. It is valid among disbelievers and among all Muslims, even between a Hanafi and a Shafi'i despite differing disposition in one aspect: intentional omission of tasmiya (Bismillah) is permissible for Shafi'i but not for Hanafi. ... For him (Abu Yousuf): the disbeliever and Muslim are equal in agency and surety, and consideration is for the increase of disposition that one possesses without the other. For them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): there is no equality between Muslim and disbeliever in disposition; if a dhimmi purchases wine or pork with the capital, it is valid, but if a Muslim does so, it is not. According to Malik and Shafi'i, mufawada is not valid at all.)
Source pages p. 548

Commentary
This passage outlines the conditions for a valid mufawada partnership in the Hanafi school. The base ruling states that mufawada requires equality in three respects: freedom (both must be free), maturity (both must be adults), and religion (both must belong to the same religious community). Thus it is invalid between a free Muslim and a slave, a minor and an adult, or a Muslim and a non-Muslim. However, Abu Yusuf disagrees on the last point and holds that mufawada between a Muslim and a non-Muslim is valid. The commentator explains that among non-Muslims, all disbelief is considered one religion, so a partnership between a Jew and a Zoroastrian is valid. Similarly, divisions among Muslims do not affect validity. The basis of Abu Hanifa's position is that there is no true equality in disposition (tasarruf) between a Muslim and a non-Muslim because the non-Muslim can legally trade in prohibited items like wine, while a Muslim cannot. Abu Yusuf argues that they are equal in the capacity for agency and surety, so the partnership can operate, and any disparity is accommodated. The passage also notes that Malik and Shafi'i reject the entire concept of mufawada.

Key Terms
مفاوضة (mufawada): A type of partnership where both partners are equal in capital, agency, and surety, and all profits/losses are shared equally.
حلم (hilm): Maturity and soundness of mind; synonymous with bulugh (legal majority). Used here as a condition for partnership.
ملة (milla): Religion or religious community; the text considers Islam as one milla and disbelief as one milla.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is valid if the non-Muslim is a dhimmi (scriptuary).
C) It is never valid between a Muslim and a non-Muslim.
D) It is valid if the Muslim gives explicit permission for the non-Muslim to trade in wine.

Key Takeaway
Mufawada partnership is only valid when partners are equal in freedom, maturity, and religion; a Muslim-non-Muslim partnership is invalid according to the majority Hanafi position.

Hanafi Validation of Mufawadah Against Qiyas
Debate on validity of mufawadah between Hanafi and opposing schools

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates mufawadah despite the qiyas objection, and evaluate the Shafi'i/Maliki counter-argument.

Core Idea
Continuous customary practice (ta'amul) can override qiyas and establish a contract's validity even when its components are individually uncertain.

This passage presents a classic dispute between the Hanafi school and the Shafi'i/Maliki schools regarding the validity of mufawadah, a form of unlimited partnership. While the opposing schools reject it based on qiyas against unknown agency and suretyship, the Hanafis argue that the consistent practice of Muslims (ta'amul) functions as a consensus that overrides qiyas, supported by a prophetic hadith. In this lesson, you will analyze the arguments on both sides and understand the Hanafi methodology of prioritizing customary practice.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Mufawadah is valid and permissible according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, despite the qiyas objection.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that mufawadah is invalid (la tajuuz).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Their proof is qiyas: mufawadah includes agency (wakalah) and suretyship (kafalah) with unknown subject matter, each of which alone is invalid when the subject is unknown.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is twofold: (1) General agency is valid by consensus despite uncertainty, and mufawadah is not fully general (it excludes household necessities), so the uncertainty is not fatal. (2) The continuous customary practice (ta'amul) of Muslims without disapproval acts as a consensus that overrides qiyas. Additionally, a hadith (though weak) mentions mufawadah as blessed.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis permit mufawadah partnerships, while Malikis and Shafi'is do not, affecting the validity of many trade arrangements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند مالك والشَّافِعِي له لا تجوز المفاوضة أصلاً. ولهما : إنه لا تساوي بين المسلم والكافر تصرُّفاً ؛ فإنَّ الدمي لو اشترى برأس المال خموراً أو خنازير صح، ولو اشتراها مسلم لا يصح، كذا في «الهداية». قوله : لا تجوز...الخ ؛ وجه عدم الجواز، وهو القياس أنَّ المفاوضة تتضمّنُ الوكالة بمجهول الجنس، والكفالة بمجهول، وكلّ ذلك بانفراده فاسد، ألا ترى أنه لو قال : وكلتك بالشراء أو شراء الثّوبِ لا تصح الوكالة، وكذا الكفالة لا تصح إلا بالمعلوم. فإن قيل : الوكالة العامة جائزة اتفاقاً مع وجود الجهالة، كما إذا قال: وكلتك اصنع في مالي ما شئت. قلنا : العموم ليس بمراد في المفاوضة ، فإنّه لا تثبت الوكالة في حق شري الطعام لأهله ، فإذا لم يكن عاماً كان توكيلاً بمجهول الجنس. ونحن نقول : قد تعامل النّاسُ بهذا العقد من غير نكير، والتعامل في حكم الإجماع فيترك به القياس، وقد شهد به قوله : «ثلاث فيهنَّ البركة : البيع إلى أجل، والمفاوضة ، واختلاط البرِّ بالشعير للبيتِ لا للبيع»، أخرجه ابن ماجة.
And according to Malik and al-Shafi'i, mufawadah is not permissible at all. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): There is no equality between Muslim and disbeliever in transactions; for if a dhimmi buys with capital wines or pigs, it is valid, and if a Muslim buys them, it is not valid. Likewise in al-Hidayah. His saying: 'It is not permissible...' : The reason for non-permissibility, and it is the analogy, is that mufawadah includes agency of unknown type, and suretyship of unknown, and each of these alone is invalid. Do you not see that if he said: 'I appoint you agent for purchase' or 'purchase of the garment,' the agency is not valid, and likewise suretyship is not valid except with known (thing). If it is said: General agency is permissible by consensus despite the existence of uncertainty, as when he says: 'I appoint you agent to do whatever you wish in my wealth.' We say: Generality is not intended in mufawadah, for agency is not established regarding the purchase of food for his family. So if it is not general, it is an agency of unknown type. And we say: People have practiced this contract without disapproval, and practice is in the position of consensus, so analogy is abandoned due to it. And the saying of him (peace be upon him) bears witness to it: 'Three things in which there is blessing: credit sale, mufawadah, and mixing wheat with barley for the house not for sale.' Reported by Ibn Majah.
Source pages p. 549

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute over the validity of mufawadah (a form of unlimited partnership). The text presents the position of Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i that mufawadah is invalid. The Hanafi position, attributed to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani (hence 'lahuma'), is that it is valid. The opponent's primary argument (based on qiyas) is that mufawadah involves agency (wakalah) of unknown type and suretyship (kafalah) of unknown type, and each alone is invalid when the subject matter is unknown. For example, if one says 'I appoint you agent to buy' without specifying the item, the agency is invalid. Similarly, suretyship requires the amount to be known. The Hanafi response counters that general agency is valid by consensus despite uncertainty, but then clarifies that mufawadah is not fully general—it does not include agency for buying household food. However, the Hanafis argue that the contract's validity is established by the consistent practice of the community (ta'amul), which acts as a form of ijma (consensus) overriding the qiyas. Additionally, they cite a hadith (though weak in transmission) that mentions mufawadah as one of three blessed transactions. Thus, the Hanafi school follows this practice.

Key Terms
المفاوضة (al-mufawadah): A type of partnership where partners are equal in capital, profit, and liability, and each is agent and guarantor for the other in all business transactions.
التعامل (al-ta'amul): Customary practice of people that, when consistent, functions as evidence akin to consensus, overriding qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Hanafi proof for the validity of mufawadah against the qiyas objection?
A) A clear Quranic verse
B) Consensus of the Companions
C) Continuous customary practice (ta'amul) supported by a hadith
D) Analogy to general agency

Key Takeaway
The validity of mufawadah in the Hanafi school rests on the primacy of customary practice (ta'amul) as evidence overriding qiyas, a key feature of Hanafi legal methodology.

8 min
Mufawadah Includes Agency and Suretyship
Explaining that mufawadah entails agency and suretyship between partners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two inherent elements of mufawadah partnership and identify which debts are covered by the suretyship.

Core Idea
In mufawadah, each partner is both agent and guarantor for the other, but only for trade debts — not personal ones.

The mufawadah partnership is not just a joint investment; it establishes a deep legal bond where each partner acts as the other's agent in trade and as a guarantor for trade-related debts. This lesson unpacks the scope of these mutual rights and obligations, clarifying the boundary between what is shared and what remains personal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two inclusions
Recognize that mufawadah inherently includes agency (wakalah) and suretyship (kafalah). Understand why these are essential for the partnership to function: agency ensures joint ownership of trade acquisitions, and suretyship ensures equality in liability.
These are not separate contracts but consequences of the mufawadah agreement.
2
Understand the scope of agency
Agency covers all trade transactions (purchase, sale, hiring) but excludes personal necessities like food and clothing for the partner's own family, and a slave-girl for intercourse (with permission). These are exceptions based on circumstantial evidence.
The exception is justified because the circumstances indicate the purchase is personal, not for the partnership.
3
Understand the scope of suretyship
Suretyship covers debts arising from transactions valid for partnership, such as purchase, sale, and hiring. It does not cover debts from criminal acts (jinayah), marriage, khul', compromise for intentional killing, or maintenance (nafaqah).
The key criterion is whether the debt arises from a trade-related contract.
4
Note the practical consequence
When one partner buys trade goods, the seller can claim the price from either partner directly: the buyer primarily and the other partner as guarantor. The guarantor then has recourse against the buyer for his share.
This ensures creditors can easily recover, and the partnership credit remains strong.
5
Confirm the exceptions
Review the exceptions mentioned in the text: food for family, clothing, and slave-girl for intercourse (with permission). Also confirm that debts from marriage, crimes, etc., are not guaranteed.
These exceptions preserve personal autonomy and prevent unfair burden on the partner.

And it includes agency and suretyship: i.e., each one is agent of the other in transactions, and likewise each one is guarantor for the other. As for agency, it is to achieve the purpose, and that is: and everything purchased belongs to both of them, except food for his family and their clothing, and every debt incurred by one of them in matters where partnership is valid, such as purchase, sale, and hiring. So if one of them buys something, the seller can demand the price from the other partner. [2] And everything purchased belongs to both of them, except food for his family, and their clothing, and every debt incurred by one of them in matters where partnership is valid, such as purchase, sale, and hiring. Partnership is in wealth. As for suretyship, it is to achieve equality in what pertains to trade, and that is the right of claim and the like. [11] His saying: 'For the seller'; i.e., he can demand the price from whichever he wishes: the buyer primarily and his partner by suretyship, and the guarantor has recourse against the buyer for his share of what he paid, because he paid a debt owed by him from wealth shared between them, as in al-Hidayah. [2] His saying: 'And everything purchased' is in the passive voice: i.e., whatever one buys becomes shared between them, and excepted from that is what is for his needs such as food for his family, clothing for them, and a slave-girl for intercourse with the permission of his partner, because what is known by indication of circumstance is like what is stipulated by speech. [3] His saying: 'And every debt' is a subject whose predicate is his coming statement 'the other guarantees it', and the clause 'incurred by one of them' means one of the mufawid partners is a description of the debt. He said in al-Hidayah: 'Whatever debts are incurred by each of them as a substitute for what partnership is valid in, the other is a guarantor for it to achieve equality. So among what partnership is valid in: purchase, sale, and hiring. And from the other category: injury (jirah), marriage, khul', compromise for intentional killing, and maintenance.' End. And in al-Binayah: As for the example of sale and purchase, it is apparent, but in a valid sale the price is due, and in a defective one the value. And the example of hiring is that one of the mufawid partners hires an employee in their trade or an animal or something, so the hirer can take the wage from whichever he wishes, because hiring is one of the contracts of trade, and each one is a guarantor for his partner regarding what is incurred from trade.
Source pages p. 549

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the ruling that mufawadah (unlimited partnership) includes two elements: agency (wakalah) and suretyship (kafalah). This means that each partner in a mufawadah contract acts as an agent for the other in business transactions, and also as a guarantor for the other's debts arising from such transactions. The text explains that the purpose of agency is that purchases made by either partner are owned jointly by both, except for personal necessities like food and clothing for the partner's family and a slave-girl for sexual relations (with permission). These exceptions are excluded because they are understood by context to be personal. Similarly, any debt incurred by one partner in a transaction valid for partnership (such as purchase, sale, or hiring) becomes a joint liability; the other partner is a guarantor. For example, if one partner buys goods, the seller can demand payment from either partner: directly from the buyer or from the other partner as guarantor. The guarantor then has recourse against the buyer for his share. The text cites al-Hidayah and al-Binayah to further clarify that not all debts are covered—only those arising from trade-related contracts. Debts from crimes (jinayah), marriage, khul', compromise for intentional killing, and maintenance (nafaqah) are not included in the partnership's guarantee. The ruling ensures equality between partners in trade, which is the foundation of mufawadah.

Key Terms
الوكالة (al-wakalah): In mufawadah, each partner is an agent for the other in trade transactions, so purchases and sales benefit both.
الكفالة (al-kafalah): Suretyship; each partner guarantees debts arising from trade contracts of the other, allowing the creditor to claim from either.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A debt from purchasing trade goods
B) A debt from marriage mahr
C) A debt from diyah for unintentional killing
D) All debts without exception

Key Takeaway
In mufawadah, each partner is both agent and guarantor for the other, but only for trade-related transactions — personal debts are excluded.

Shared Purchases in Mufawada Partnership
Establishes the rule of shared ownership and an exception for personal necessities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule of shared purchases in a mufawada partnership and identify the exception for personal necessities, explaining the basis of contextual indication.

Core Idea
In a mufawada partnership, every trade-related purchase is jointly owned, but personal necessities are excepted.

A fundamental rule in the Hanafi school of partnership (shirkah) is that any purchase made by one partner in a valid mufawada (unlimited) partnership becomes the property of both. However, this rule has a crucial exception: items bought for personal or family necessities are not shared. This lesson unpacks the rule, its exception, and the reasoning behind it, based on the classical text al-Wiqayah and its commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the passage is ruling on: the purchase of goods by one partner in a mufawada partnership. The ruling is that the purchased item becomes jointly owned by both partners.
This is the default rule for all purchases related to trade.
2
Note the exception
Identify the exception: purchases that are personal necessities for the partner, such as food for his family and their clothing, are not shared. Also, the commentary adds a slave-girl for intercourse with implicit permission.
The exception is based on the fact that such items are not considered part of the partnership's trade activities.
3
Understand the basis of the exception
The exception is justified by 'contextual indication' (dalalat al-hal): the partner's implicit consent is assumed for necessities, so it is as if it were stipulated verbally. This principle treats known contextual situations as explicit conditions.
This is a key Hanafi usul principle applied in fiqh.
4
Apply the rule to new scenarios
Practice distinguishing between trade-related purchases (shared) and personal ones (not shared). Consider factors like the nature of the item and the intention at the time of purchase.
Modern examples: inventory, raw materials, office supplies vs. groceries, personal clothing, family car.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومشترى كلّ لهما، إلا طعام أهله وكسوتهم، وكلُّ دينِ لَزِمَ أحدهما بما تصحُ فيه الشركة، كالشراء، والبيع، والاستئجار. [٢] ومشترى كلّ لهما، إلا طعام أهله، وكسوتهم، وكل دين لَزِمَ أحدهما بما تصح فيه الشركة، كالشراء، والبيع، والاستئجار) الشركة في المال، وأما الكفالة فلتحقق المساواة فيما هو من موح التجارات، وهو توجه المطالبة ونحوهما. قوله : فللبائع ; أي له أن يطالب بالثمن أيهما شاء : المشتري بالأصالة وصاحبه بالكفالة، ويرجعُ الكفيل على المشتري بحصته بما أدى ; لأنه قضى ديناً عليه من مال مشترك بينهما، كذا في «الهداية) ٢ قوله : ومشترى ; بصيغة المجهول : أي ما اشتراه أحدهما يكون مشتركاً بينهما، ويستثنى منه ما كان من حوائجه كالطعام لأهله، والكسوة لهم، والجارية للوطء بإذنِ شريكه ; لأنَّ المعلوم بدلالة الحال كالمشترط بالمقال.
And what is purchased belongs to both, except food for his family and their clothing, and every debt incurred by one of them in what partnership is valid in, such as purchase, sale, and hire. [2] And what is purchased belongs to both, except food for his family and their clothing, and every debt incurred by one of them in what partnership is valid in, such as purchase, sale, and hire. Partnership in wealth, and as for suretyship, it is to achieve equality in what is from the matters of trade, which is the direction of claim and the like. His statement: 'For the seller' means that he has the right to demand the price from whichever he wishes: the buyer primarily and his partner as surety, and the surety recovers from the buyer his share of what he paid, because he paid a debt owed by him from wealth shared between them, as in al-Hidayah. His statement: 'And what is purchased' (passive voice) means that what one purchases becomes shared between them, and excepted from that is what is from his necessities like food for his family, clothing for them, and a slave-girl for intercourse with the permission of his partner, because that which is known by contextual indication is like that which is stipulated verbally.
Source pages p. 550

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of al-Wiqayah, explained by the commentator. It establishes the principle that in a mufawada (unlimited) partnership, any item purchased by one partner becomes jointly owned by both partners. However, there is an exception: items that are personal necessities for the purchasing partner, such as food for his family and clothing for them, are not shared. The commentator clarifies that the exception also includes a slave-girl purchased for sexual relations, provided the partner's permission is obtained implicitly through context. The reason is that the partner's consent implied by the situation (dalalat al-hal) is treated as if it were explicitly stipulated. The commentator also explains that the seller can demand the full price from either partner: the buyer primarily and the other as surety (kafil). The paying partner can then recover half from the buyer.

Key Terms
مفاوضة (mufāwaḍah): A type of partnership where each partner is an agent and surety for the other in all matters of trade, and they share all profits and losses equally.
كفالة (kafālah): Suretyship; here, each partner becomes a surety for the other regarding debts arising from trade.
دلالة الحال (dalālat al-ḥāl): Contextual indication; the meaning understood from the circumstances, treated as an implicit stipulation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Raw materials for manufacturing

Key Takeaway
In a mufawada partnership, trade-related purchases are automatically shared, but personal necessities are excepted based on contextual indication.

Debts Included in Mufawada Partnership Liability
Lists debts shared in mufawada and those excluded

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between shared and personal debts in mufawada partnership and apply the rule to new scenarios.

Core Idea
In mufawada, only trade-related debts are shared; personal and criminal liabilities remain individual.

Mufawada partnership is founded on equality of capital and liability. This principle governs which debts are shared between partners. The text from al-Hidayah and its commentaries clarifies that only debts incurred as substitutes for trade activities (sale, purchase, hire) bind both partners, while liabilities from personal acts such as marriage, criminal injury, or maintenance remain individual.

Guided Path
1
Identify the underlying principle
Understand that mufawada requires equality (musawa) in liability; this drives which debts are shared.
The principle is stated explicitly in al-Hidayah: '...to achieve equality.'
2
List shared debt categories
From the text: debts from sale, purchase, hire—any transaction valid for partnership.
Also debts resembling trade (borrowing, usurpation, consumption, etc.) are included by extension.
3
List excluded debt categories
Jinaya (criminal injury), nikah (marriage), khul', sulh for intentional homicide, nafaqa (maintenance) are personal.
These are explicitly excluded because they are not from trade.
4
Understand the extension to trade-like debts
Debts from borrowing, usurpation, consumption, denied trust, loan for use are shared because liability there resembles trade.
This expansion is cited from al-Bahr and other works.
5
Apply to scenarios
Given a specific debt, determine if it arises from a trade transaction or resembles trade; otherwise it is personal.
Use the categories from steps 2-3 as a checklist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
