[١] قوله : كالجناية ... الخ ; قال في «العناية : فلو ادعى رجل على أحد المتفاوضين جراحة خطأ لها أرش مقدّر ، واستحلفه فحلف ليس له أن يستحلف شريكه ولا خصومة له معه، وكذا المهر والخلعُ والصلح عن جناية العبد. والصلح عن النفقة إذا ادعاء على أحدهما وحلّفه ليس له أن يحلف الآخر; لأنها ليست من التجارة، وصورة الخلع ما إذا كانت المرأة فاوضت ثم خالعت مع زوجها ، فما لزم عليها من بدل الخلع لا يلزم شريكها ، وكذلك لو أقرت ببدل الخلع. [٢] قوله : والنكاح ; فلو نكح أحدهما ووجب عليه المهر لا يؤاخذ به الآخر، ولو خالعت المفاوضة زوجها لا يؤاخذ الآخر ببدل الخلع ، ولو قتل أحدهما إنساناً عمداً ووجب عليه القصاص، فصالح مع ورثة المقتول على المال فهو عليه خاصةً لا على شریکه. [٣] قوله : وكالنفقة ; أشار بزيادة الكاف إلى كونه معطوفاً على الجناية، فنفقة الزوجة وغيرها من الأقارب الواجبة على أحدهما لا تجب على شريكه ; لأنها ليست من أمور التجارة.
[3] His statement: 'And every debt' - it is a subject, its predicate is his statement later 'the other is surety for it', and the clause 'incurred by one of them' meaning one of the two mufawada partners is an adjective for 'debt'. He said in al-Hidayah: 'What each of them incurs of debts as a substitute for what partnership is valid in, the other is a guarantor for it to achieve equality. So from what partnership is valid in: purchase, sale, and hire. And from the other category: criminal injury, marriage, khul', compromise for intentional bloodshed, and for maintenance.' End. And in al-Binayah: 'As for the case of sale and purchase, it is apparent, but in a valid sale the price is owed, and in a corrupt sale the value is owed. And the case of hire is that if one of the mufawada partners hires a worker in their trade or an animal or something, then the hirer can take the wage from whichever he wishes, because hire is a trade contract, and each one is a surety for his partner in what is imposed on him from trade.' Or by suretyship with a command - it contains a caution against the imposition of a debt due to a cause in which partnership is not valid: such as criminal injury, marriage, khul', compromise for intentional bloodshed, and maintenance. And it is mentioned in al-Bahr, Manh al-Ghaffar, and others: 'Debt that resembles trade guarantee is like debt from trade, so it includes debt due to borrowing, usurpation, consumption, denied or consumed trust, and likewise loan for use, because the establishment of liability in these cases benefits that principle, so it becomes in the meaning of trade.' [1] His statement: 'such as criminal injury' etc.; he said in al-Inayah: 'So if a man claims against one of the mufawada partners a mistaken wound with an assessed compensation, and he administers an oath to him and he swears, he cannot administer an oath to his partner and has no litigation with him. Likewise, the dower, khul', and compromise for a slave's injury. And compromise for maintenance - if he claims it against one of them and administers an oath to him, he cannot administer an oath to the other, because they are not from trade. And the case of khul' is when a woman has a mufawada partnership and then she makes khul' with her husband, then what is imposed on her of the khul' compensation does not become obligatory on her partner, and likewise if she acknowledges the khul' compensation.' [2] His statement: 'and marriage'; so if one of them marries and the dower becomes obligatory on him, the other is not held liable for it; and if the mufawada woman makes khul' with her husband, the other partner is not held liable for the khul' compensation; and if one of them kills a person intentionally and retaliation becomes obligatory on him, and he compromises with the heirs of the slain for property, it is upon him alone, not on his partner. [3] His statement: 'and maintenance'; he indicated by the extra kaf that it is conjoined to criminal injury, so the maintenance of the wife and other relatives obligatory upon one of them does not become obligatory on his partner, because they are not from matters of trade.
Source pages p. 550

Commentary
This passage is a detailed expansion of the rule regarding debts in mufawada partnership. The commentator, quoting al-Hidayah, explains that debts incurred as a substitute for a transaction in which partnership is valid (such as purchase, sale, hire) bind both partners to achieve equality. Debts from other causes, such as criminal injury (jinaya), marriage (nikah), khul', compromise for intentional homicide, and maintenance (nafaqa), are not shared. The commentator further clarifies each category: for example, in a valid sale, the price is owed; in a corrupt sale, the market value; in hire, the hirer can claim the wage from either partner. The passage also cites other works (al-Bahr, Manh al-Ghaffar) that expand the scope of shared debts to include those resembling trade, such as from borrowing, usurpation, consumption, denied trust, etc., because they effectively carry a trade-like liability. The sub-commentaries then give specific examples: e.g., if one partner is sued for a wound, the other partner cannot be sued; if one marries, the dower is solely his responsibility; if one commits murder and compromises, the payment is solely his; and maintenance obligations are personal.

Key Terms
جناية (jināyah): Criminal act causing harm, such as wounding or killing, which incurs liability for compensation or retaliation.
خلع (khulʿ): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation paid to the husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Price of goods purchased for trade
B) Wage of a worker hired for the business
C) Compensation for accidentally injuring someone
D) Rent for a shop used in partnership

Key Takeaway
In mufawada, only debts stemming from trade or trade-like transactions bind the other partner; personal and criminal liabilities are individual.

Kafalah with Command in Mufawada: The Sahibayn’s Objection
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafalah liability in mufawada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding kafalah with command in mufawada and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Kafalah is tabarru', so debts from it are not shared in mufawada, according to the Sahibayn.

In a mufawada partnership, partners generally share all trade-related liabilities equally. However, when one partner acts as a surety (kafalah) at the request of the principal debtor, an intra-Hanafi dispute arises: does this debt bind the other partner? Imam Abu Hanifa considered it joint, but his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagreed, arguing that kafalah is a gratuitous act (tabarru') and thus not a trade transaction. This lesson examines the reasoning behind each view and the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and the point of dispute
The act is kafalah (suretyship) undertaken by one mufawada partner at the command (bi-amr) of the principal debtor. The dispute is whether the resulting debt becomes a joint liability of both partners.
Mufawada partnership implies full mutual agency and shared profits/losses in trade.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that the debt is joint, implying that kafalah in a trade context is encompassed by the partnership's scope.
His reasoning is not explicit in this passage but relies on the principle of mutual agency.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that the debt is not joint because kafalah is a gratuitous contract (tabarru'), distinct from exchange contracts (mu'awada). As such, it lies outside the scope of the partnership.
The Arabic: لأن الكفالة تبرع.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If Abu Hanifa's view is adopted, the creditor may claim from either partner. If the Sahibayn's view is adopted, only the surety partner is liable, protecting the other partner from an obligation he did not incur.
This difference affects risk allocation in mufawada.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative position)
Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., in al-Hidayah) generally prefer the Sahibayn's view, because kafalah is inherently a voluntary, non-compensatory act. Thus, the debt remains personal.
The mufta bihi aligns with the weaker party's protection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٤] قوله : أو بكفالة ; عطف على قوله : بما تصح فيه الشركة ; أي الدين الذي لزم أحدهما بكفالته بأمر المكفول عنه يلزمُ الآخر ، وهذا عنده، وقالا : لا يلزم الآخر; لأن الكفالة تبرع.
[4] His statement: 'or by suretyship' - it is conjoined to his statement: 'in what partnership is valid in'; meaning that the debt which becomes obligatory on one of them by his suretyship at the command of the principal debtor becomes obligatory on the other. This is according to him (Abu Hanifa). And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It does not become obligatory on the other, because suretyship is a gratuitous act.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the liability in mufawada partnership for debts arising from kafalah (suretyship) that was undertaken at the request (bi-amr) of the principal debtor. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that such a debt is jointly shared, reasoning that kafalah in the context of trade supports the equality of partners. However, the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree, arguing that kafalah is essentially a gratuitous contract (tabarru'), not a trade transaction, and therefore the debt remains personal and does not bind the other partner. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is typically the view of the Sahibayn in such matters of kafalah.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafālah): Suretyship; guaranteeing another's debt or obligation.
مفوضة (mufāwaḍah): A form of partnership with full mutual agency and shared liability.
تبرع (tabarruʿ): A gratuitous act, done without expectation of compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because kafalah is not a valid contract
B) Because kafalah is a gratuitous act
C) Because the principal debtor did not authorize it
D) Because kafalah is only for personal loans

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, kafalah with command does not create joint liability in mufawada because it is a gratuitous contract (tabarru').

Liabilities in Mufawada: Debts from Non-Commercial Causes
Lists non-commercial debts and discusses kafala dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which debts bind a partner in a mufawada partnership and distinguish between commercial and non-commercial causes, including the effect of order in guarantees.

Core Idea
In mufawada, only commercial debts bind both partners; personal debts like dower and criminal fines remain individual.

This lesson examines the boundaries of shared liability in the Hanafi mufawada (equal) partnership. While mufawada makes each partner an agent and guarantor in commercial matters, not all debts incurred by one partner automatically bind the other. We will explore the key categories of debts that remain personal and the nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute concerning guarantees (kafala).

Guided Path
1
Identify the governing principle
Determine the foundation of liability in mufawada: partnership is based on commerce, so only debts from commercial causes are shared.
The text contrasts debts 'due to a cause in which partnership is valid' with those that are not.
2
List the excluded non-commercial debts
Go through the examples given: jinaya (criminal injury), nikah (marriage dower), khul' (divorce consideration), sulh for intentional homicide, nafaqa (maintenance). Each is personal to the incurring partner.
The text uses 'ka' (like) for each, and includes detailed scenarios from 'al-Inayah'.
3
Analyze debts resembling trade
Note that debts from borrowing, usurpation, consumed trust, etc., are treated as commercial because they involve a liability that resembles trade.
These are binding on both partners; the text cites 'al-Bahr' and 'Minh al-Ghaffar'.
4
Examine the kafala (guarantee) dispute
Understand the intra-Hanafi disagreement: Abu Hanifa holds that a guarantee with the order of the debtor binds the other partner; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, viewing kafala as purely gratuitous.
The text explains Abu Hanifa's reasoning: kafala is initially gratuitous but becomes compensatory at the end (the guarantor can reclaim from the debtor).
5
Determine the mufta bihi (relied-upon view)
The relied-upon view, as chosen by later scholars like Abu al-Layth, differentiates: with order it binds, without order it does not.
This position is from Abu Hanifa and is considered the sound (sahih) view.

Source Text

أو بكفالة بأمر
فيه احتراز عن لزو دين بسبب لا تصح فيه الشركة : كالجناية، والنكاح"، والخلع، والصلح عن دم عمد، وكالنفقة ، أو بكفالة" بأمر
وذكر في «البحر»، و«منح الغفّار ) وغيرهما : إن الدين بما يشبه ضمان النجارة مثل الدين بالتجارة، فيدخل فيه الدِّينُ بسبب الاستقراض والغصب والاستهلاكِ والوديعة المجحودة أو المستهلكة، وكذا العارية؛ لأن تقرّر الضمان في هذه المواضع يفيد ذلك الأصل، فيصير في معنى التجارة.
[١] قوله : كالجناية ... الخ ؛ قال في «العناية : فلو ادعى رجل على أحد (۳) المتفاوضين جراحة خطأ لها أرش مقدّر ، واستحلفه فحلف ليس له أن يستحلف شريكه ولا خصومة له معه، وكذا المهر والخلعُ والصلح عن جناية العبد.
والصلح عن النفقة إذا ادعاء على أحدهما وحلّفه ليس له أن يحلف الآخر؛ لأنها ليست من التجارة، وصورة الخلع ما إذا كانت المرأة فاوضت ثم خالعت مع زوجها ، فما لزم عليها من بدل الخلع لا يلزم شريكها ، وكذلك لو أقرت ببدل الخلع.

[٣] قوله : وكالنفقة ؛ أشار بزيادة الكاف إلى كونه معطوفاً على الجناية، فنفقة الزوجة وغيرها من الأقارب الواجبة على أحدهما لا تجب على شريكه ؛ لأنها ليست من أمور التجارة.

ضمنه الآخر، وبغير أمر لا هو الصحيح ): أي إذا لَزِمَ أحدهما دين بسبب الكفالة من غير أمر المكفول عنه، فالصحيح أن هذا الدِّينَ لا يضمنه الشريك الآخر، فإن كان بأمر المكفول عنه يضمنه الشريك الآخر.

Book of Partnership: Its Types and Rulings. Or by guarantee with order. It contains avoidance of a debt due to a cause in which partnership is not valid: like criminal injury, marriage, khul', compromise for premeditated homicide, like maintenance, or by guarantee with order. It was mentioned in 'al-Bahr', 'Minh al-Ghaffar' and others: Indeed, debt that resembles the liability of commerce is like debt from trade, so it includes debt due to borrowing, usurpation, consumption, denied or consumed trust, and likewise loan for use; because the establishment of liability in these places benefits that origin, so it becomes in the meaning of trade. [1] His statement: 'like criminal injury... etc.'; he said in 'al-Inayah': If a man claimed against one of the two mufawada partners a mistaken injury with a fixed indemnity, and he made him take an oath and he swore, he may not make his partner take an oath nor has he any litigation with him, and likewise the dower, khul', and compromise for injury of a slave. And compromise for maintenance if he claimed it against one of them and made him take an oath, he may not make the other take an oath because they are not from trade. And the form of khul' is when a woman is in mufawada and then she made khul' with her husband, so whatever becomes due upon her of the khul' consideration does not bind her partner, and likewise if she acknowledged the khul' consideration. [2] His statement: 'and marriage'; so if one of them marries and the dower becomes due, the other is not held liable for it, and if the mufawada woman made khul' with her husband, the other is not held liable for the khul' consideration, and if one of them kills a person intentionally and retaliation becomes due, and he makes a compromise with the heirs of the slain for wealth, it is upon him specifically, not upon his partner. [3] His statement: 'like maintenance'; he indicated by the extra 'ka' that it is connected to criminal injury, so maintenance of wife and other relatives obligatory upon one of them does not become obligatory upon his partner because it is not from trade matters. [4] His statement: 'or by guarantee'; is connected to his statement: 'in which partnership is valid'; i.e., the debt that becomes due to one of them by his guarantee with the order of the guaranteed person binds the other, and this is according to him (Abu Hanifa), and they two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: it does not bind the other because guarantee is a gratuitous act. 'Binds the other, and without order no, that is the correct (view)': i.e., if a debt becomes due to one of them due to guarantee without the order of the guaranteed person, the correct view is that this debt is not guaranteed by the other partner; if it was with the order of the guaranteed person, the other partner guarantees it. And for him: it is a gratuitous act at the beginning and a compensatory act at the end, because the guarantor can claim indemnity from the guaranteed person if it was with his order, as opposed to guarantee of the person (bail) which is gratuitous at the beginning and end, and likewise guarantee of wealth without order, so the other is not bound due to the absence of the meaning of compensation, as in 'al-Fath'. [1] His statement: 'binds the other'; its meaning is that the plaintiff-creditor may litigate with the non-direct partner and take his debt from him. [2] His statement: 'that is the correct (view)'; he said in 'al-Fath': it indicates a difference of the scholars, and what the author (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned is the choice of Faqih Abu al-Layth (may Allah have mercy on him), and he interpreted the absolute permissibility of 'al-Jami' al-Saghir' upon it.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof on the types of debts that do and do not bind the partner in a mufawada (equal) partnership. The key principle is that partnership in mufawada is based on commercial activity; therefore, debts arising from non-commercial causes remain personal to the partner who incurred them. The text first lists such causes: jinaya (criminal injury), nikah (marriage dower), khul' (divorce by redemption), sulh for intentional homicide, and nafaqa (maintenance). Each is exemplified: for jinaya, if one partner causes injury, only he is sued; for nikah, the dower is personal; for khul', the consideration is personal; for nafaqa, family maintenance is not shared. Then it treats kafala (guarantee): if a partner guarantees a debt with the order of the guaranteed person, that debt binds the other partner according to Abu Hanifa, because the guarantee is compensatory at the end (the guarantor can reclaim from the guaranteed). But Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it does not bind because guarantee is initially gratuitous. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's when the guarantee is by order, and the latter's when without order. The text also clarifies that debts resembling trade (like borrowing, usurpation, consumed trust) do bind the partner because they fall under the meaning of trade.

Key Terms
مفاوضة (mufāwaḍah): An equal partnership where each partner is both agent and guarantor for the other in all commercial matters.
كفالة (kafālah): A guarantee or suretyship where one person becomes liable for a debt of another.
جناية (jināyah): Criminal injury causing bodily harm, for which indemnity (arsh) is due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because all debts are shared in mufawada.
B) No, because marriage and dower are not commercial transactions.
C) Yes, if the marriage was for business purposes.
D) No, unless the other partner consented to the marriage.

Key Takeaway
In mufawada partnership, only debts arising from commercial activities (or those resembling trade) are shared; personal liabilities such as dower, criminal indemnity, and maintenance remain individual, and guarantee debts bind only if made with the debtor's order (per Abu Hanifa).

When Mufawada Becomes 'Inan: Effect of Additional Wealth
Ruling on transformation of mufawada to 'inan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a mufawada partner gains extra wealth, explain why it transforms into 'inan, and identify the condition that is broken.

Core Idea
When equality is lost in mufawada, it becomes 'inan — the partnership continues but in a new form.

Mufawada partnership requires strict equality in capital and liability. But what happens if one partner suddenly receives extra property through inheritance or gift? Does the partnership dissolve entirely, or does it adjust? This passage clarifies that the partnership does not end — it simply transforms into an 'inan (limited) partnership, preserving continuity while adapting to the new capital reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger event
Look for the events that cause the transformation: inheritance, gift, or charity that gives one partner additional wealth. The partner must take possession (qabd).
The extra wealth must be of a type in which partnership is valid (i.e., tradeable property).
2
Recall the condition for mufawada
Mufawada requires complete equality (tasawi) between partners in initial capital and liability. This is a key condition.
If equality is absent at formation, the partnership is 'inan from the start.
3
Determine the consequence of broken equality
When extra wealth reaches one partner, equality is lost. The mufawada becomes invalid (batil) as a mufawada, but it does not nullify the partnership entirely.
The partnership automatically converts to 'inan.
4
Understand the nature of 'inan
'Inan partnership does not require equality. It is a limited partnership where each partner contributes what they have and is not a guarantor for the other beyond their share.
The partnership continues with the new capital proportions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ورث أحدهما، أو وهب له ما يصح فيه الشركة، وقَبَضَ صارت عناناً
(عنانا)
[٣] قوله : وإن ورث... الخ ؛ يعني إذا ورث أحد الشريكين المفاوضين ما لا تصح
فيه الشركة ؛ بأن مات مورثه فوصل إليه ،مال، أو وَهَبَ له رجلٌ مالاً تصح فيه الشركةُ وقبضه، وكذا إذا تصدق عليه وقبضه.
ففي هذه الصور تبطل المفاوضة وتصيرُ شركةُ عنان ؛ لأنَّ المفاوضة يشترط فيها تساوي الشريكين فيما تصح فيه الشركة ، فإذا فات ذلك بوصول مال زائد إلى أحدهما بطلت، وشركةُ عنان لا يشترط فيه التّساوي على ما سيجيء تفصيله.
And if one of them inherits, or is given a gift of something in which partnership is valid, and takes possession, it becomes an 'inan partnership. ('Inan). [3] His statement: 'And if he inherits... etc.'; meaning: if one of the two mufawada partners inherits wealth that is not valid for partnership? Actually the text says 'ما لا تصح فيه الشركة' but it seems a contradiction; the explanation says 'or a man gives him wealth in which partnership is valid'. The intended meaning: if one partner receives extra wealth via inheritance or gift, the mufawada is nullified and becomes 'inan. Because mufawada requires equality in that which partnership is valid, and when that is lost due to additional wealth reaching one of them, it becomes invalid, and 'inan partnership does not require equality, as will be detailed.

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: if one partner in a mufawada partnership receives additional property through inheritance, gift, or charity and takes possession, the mufawada partnership dissolves and transforms into an 'inan (limited) partnership. The reason is that mufawada requires complete equality between partners in capital and liability; when one partner gains extra wealth, equality is broken. 'Inan partnership, on the other hand, does not require equality. The text clarifies that the additional wealth must be of a type in which partnership is valid (i.e., commercial property). The transformation occurs automatically upon possession, and the partnership continues as 'inan with the new capital proportions.

Key Terms
عنان ('inān): A limited partnership where partners contribute capital but are not full guarantors for each other.
مفاوضة (mufāwaḍah): An equal partnership with mutual agency and guarantee.
تساوي (tasāwī): Equality of partners in capital and liability, a condition for mufawada.

In Hanafi fiqh, a mufawada partnership transforms into an 'inan partnership when one partner gains extra wealth through inheritance, gift, or charity, because the condition of equality is broken.

Liability for Surety Debt in Mufawadah Partnership
Ruling with proof on partner liability in mufawadah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on partner liability for a co-partner's kafalah in a mufawadah partnership and distinguish when command (amr) triggers liability.

Core Idea
A partner is not liable for the other's surety debt unless the surety was commanded by the principal debtor.

In a mufawadah partnership, partners share authority and liability broadly. But does that mean one partner is automatically liable for any debt the other incurs through suretyship? This passage clarifies the precise condition: the partner is shielded from liability unless the surety was given with the command of the other partner. Understanding this distinction is crucial for real partnership transactions.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the ruling: the other partner is not liable (la yadman) for a debt arising from kafalah without command. If there is command, he is liable.
The text uses 'al-sahih' to indicate the correct view.
2
Understand the reasoning
Recognize the legal basis: kafalah with command is initially a donation (tabarru') but ends as a compensatory transaction (mu'awadah) because the surety can claim recourse. Without command, it remains a pure donation, so no compensation element binds the partner.
Kafalah for the person is always a donation, even with command.
3
Identify the dispute
Note that there is a difference among early scholars (mashayikh). The author follows Abu al-Layth's view, which restricts the absolute permissibility of al-Jami' al-Saghir to cases with command.
The dispute shows the need for tarjih (preference).
4
Apply the ruling
In practice: if Partner A becomes surety for a debt without Partner B's command, the creditor cannot sue Partner B. If Partner A acted with Partner B's command, Partner B is jointly liable.
This protects the non-consenting partner from unexpected debts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضمنه الآخر، وبغير أمر لا هو الصحيح (١) أي إذا لَزِمَ أحدهما دين بسبب الكفالة من غير أمر المكفول عنه، فالصحيح أن هذا الدِّينَ لا يضمنه الشريك الآخر، فإن كان بأمر المكفول عنه يضمنه الشريك الآخر. وله : إنها تبرع ابتداء، ومعاوضة انتهاء، فإنَّ للكفيل تضمين المكفول عنه لو كانت بأمره بخلاف كفالة النفس فإنَّها تبرع ابتداءً وانتهاءً، وكذا كفالة المال بلا أمر فلا يلزم الآخر؛ لانعدام معنى المعاوضة، كذا في «الفتح»). [١]قوله : ضمنه الآخر ؛ معناه أنَّ للمدعي الدائن أن يخاصم مع الشريك الغيرِ المباشر ويأخذ منه دينه. [٢] قوله : هو الصحيح ؛ قال في «الفتح» (۳) : يشير إلى خلاف المشايخ، وما ذكره المصنف الله مختار الفقيه أبي الليث الله ، وحمل مطلق جواز «الجامع الصغير عليه.
The other partner guarantees it, and without command the correct view is that he does not. (1) Meaning: if a debt becomes binding on one of them due to suretyship without the command of the principal debtor, then the correct view is that this debt is not guaranteed by the other partner. If it was with the command of the principal debtor, the other partner guarantees it. And he has: that it is a donation initially and a compensation in the end, for the surety has recourse against the principal if it was with his command, unlike suretyship for the person which is a donation initially and finally, and likewise suretyship for property without command does not bind the other due to the absence of the meaning of compensation, as in al-Fath. [1] He said: 'The other partner guarantees it' meaning that the plaintiff creditor may litigate with the other partner who is not the direct one and take his debt from him. [2] He said: 'It is the correct view' — he said in al-Fath: it indicates a difference of the mashayikh, and what the author mentioned is the choice of the jurist Abu al-Layth, and he applies the absolute permissibility of al-Jami' al-Saghir to it.
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage states a ruling on liability in a mufawadah partnership when one partner becomes a surety (kafalah) for a debt. The correct view (al-sahih) is that if the surety was undertaken without the command of the other partner, the other partner is not liable; but if it was with his command, he is liable. The commentary explains that a surety for property with command is a donation initially but becomes a compensatory transaction due to the right of recourse, whereas a surety for the person remains a donation entirely. The text then clarifies that the phrase 'the other guarantees it' means the creditor can sue the non-direct partner and recover his debt. Finally, it notes that this ruling resolves a difference among earlier scholars (mashayikh) and aligns with the choice of Imam Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi, who applied the general permissibility from al-Jami' al-Saghir.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where one party (the surety) guarantees a debt or obligation of another (the principal debtor).
مفاوضة (mufawadah): A type of partnership where each partner has full authority and liability, and they share equally in all profits and losses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a mufawadah partnership, if one partner becomes a surety (kafalah) for a debt without the command of the other partner, what is the correct view regarding the other partner's liability?
A) The other partner is liable because partnership implies mutual guarantee.
B) The other partner is not liable because suretyship without command lacks the meaning of compensation.
C) The other partner is liable only if the debt is for trade.
D) The other partner is liable proportionally to his share in the partnership.

Key Takeaway
A mufawadah partner is not liable for the other's kafalah unless the surety was given with the partner's command; without command, the surety remains a donation and does not bind the partnership.

Inheritance or Gift Transforms Mufawadah to 'Inan
Ruling on change of partnership type due to inheritance or gift

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a mufawadah partnership converts to an 'inan partnership upon a partner receiving additional property through inheritance or gift, and apply the distinction between cash-like property and goods/real estate.

Core Idea
A mufawadah partnership breaks when one partner gains extra cash-like property, but goods and real estate have no effect.

In a mufawadah partnership, absolute equality of capital and liability is essential. If one partner acquires additional wealth through inheritance or a gift that is valid for partnership (cash), the equality is lost and the mufawadah becomes invalid. However, the relationship does not dissolve entirely; it automatically transforms into an 'inan partnership, which tolerates inequality. This passage explains the nuances: only property in the form of cash or its equivalents triggers the conversion, and for gifts, possession is required. Goods and real estate do not affect the partnership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger event
Determine whether the partner received property through inheritance, gift, or charity. Inheritance automatically transfers ownership; gift requires possession (qabd) to complete ownership.
If a gift is given but not physically taken, no additional wealth accrues in the partner's ownership, so the mufawadah remains.
2
Classify the received property
Check if the property is of the type that is valid for partnership in mufawadah. According to the Hanafi school, only cash (gold, silver) and what takes their place qualify. Goods ('ard) and real estate ('aqar) are not part of the mufawadah capital.
The text distinguishes between 'ard and 'arad; refer to the Zakat chapter for details.
3
Assess the condition of equality
If the received property is cash-like and the partner obtains ownership, his capital now exceeds the other's, breaking the required equality. This invalidates the mufawadah contract.
Equality is a condition for the validity of mufawadah, not for 'inan.
4
Apply the consequence
Once invalidated, the relationship automatically becomes an 'inan partnership. No new contract is needed; the existing capital remains, and the partners now operate under 'inan rules (inequality allowed, no mutual suretyship).
If the property is non-cash (goods/real estate), the mufawadah continues unchanged because the partnership capital did not increase.
5
Note the case of goods and real estate
Reiterate that goods and land are not considered in the mufawadah capital, so receiving them does not affect equality, hence the mufawadah remains intact.
This is because mufawadah only concerns cash equivalents for trading purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣]قوله : وإن ورث... الخ ; يعني إذا ورث أحد الشريكين المفاوضين ما لا تصح فيه الشركة ؛ بأن مات مورثه فوصل إليه مال، أو وَهَبَ له رجلٌ مالاً تصح فيه الشركةُ وقبضه، وكذا إذا تصدق عليه وقبضه. ففي هذه الصور تبطل المفاوضة وتصيرُ شركةُ عنان ؛ لأنَّ المفاوضة يشترط فيها تساوي الشريكين فيما تصح فيه الشركة ، فإذا فات ذلك بوصول مال زائد إلى أحدهما بطلت، وشركةُ عنان لا يشترط فيه التّساوي على ما سيجيء تفصيله. وفي العرض والعقار بقيت مفاوضة (٤) [١]قوله : القبض ; أشار به إلى وجه ذكر المصنف القبض وإلى أن قوله : قبض ; مرتبط بقوله : وهب له ; لا بقوله : ورث ; وحاصله أنَّ القبض لما كان شرطاً لإفادة الهبة الملك ، ذكر المصنّف القبض ، فلو وهب مال ولم يقبضه لم تبطل المفاوضة ; لعدم حصول مال زائد في ملكه. [٢]قوله : وفي العرض ; بفتح العين المهملة وسكون الراء المهملة، يطلق على متاع غير النقدين، والعَرَض بفتحتين أعم منه ، وقد ذكرنا الفرق بينهما في «كتاب الزكاة» فتذكره. [٣] قوله : لأن مال الشركة لم يزد ; فإنَّ ما تصح فيه المفاوضة إنما هو النقدان، وما يقوم مقامهما لا غيرهما من الأمتعة والأراضي على ما سيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى.
[3] His statement: 'And if one of them inherits... etc.' meaning: if one of the two partners in a mufawadah inherits property in which partnership is not valid, i.e., his testator dies and wealth reaches him, or a man gives him a gift of property in which partnership is valid and he takes possession, and similarly if someone gives him charity and he takes possession. So in these cases the mufawadah is invalidated and it becomes an 'inan partnership, because in mufawadah equality of the partners in what is valid for partnership is a condition, so when this fails due to additional wealth reaching one of them, it is invalidated; and in 'inan partnership equality is not a condition, as will come in detail. And in the case of goods and real estate the mufawadah remains. [4] [1] His statement: 'the taking possession' — he indicated by it the aspect that the author mentioned taking possession, and that his statement 'took possession' is connected to his statement 'gave him a gift', not to his statement 'inherited'; and the gist is that since taking possession is a condition for the gift to confer ownership, the author mentioned taking possession, so if someone gives a gift and he does not take possession, the mufawadah is not invalidated because no additional wealth accrued in his ownership. [2] His statement: 'And in goods' — with a fatha on the 'ayn and sukun on the ra', it is applied to property other than the two metals (gold and silver), and 'arad with two fatha is more general; we have mentioned the difference between them in the Book of Zakat, so remember it. [3] His statement: 'Because the partnership wealth did not increase' — for that in which mufawadah is valid is only the two metals (gold and silver) and what takes their place, not other goods and lands, as will come if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when a partner in a mufawadah partnership receives additional property through inheritance or gift. The key condition for mufawadah is equality of capital and the types of property that can be part of the partnership, which are primarily gold and silver (naydayn) or their equivalents. If one partner receives property through inheritance or a gift that is valid for partnership (like cash), and he takes possession, then his wealth exceeds the other's, breaking equality. Consequently, the mufawadah becomes invalid and transforms into an 'inan partnership (where inequality is allowed). However, if the received property is goods ('ard) or real estate ('aqar), which are not considered valid for partnership in mufawadah, the mufawadah remains unaffected because such property does not increase the partnership capital. The commentary further clarifies that for a gift, possession (qabd) is a condition for invalidating mufawadah; without possession, no additional wealth accrues, so the partnership remains. The distinction between 'ard (goods other than cash) and 'arad (broader category) is noted from the Zakat book. Finally, the text emphasizes that mufawadah is only concerned with cash equivalents.

Key Terms
عنان ('inan): A partnership in trade where partners may have unequal capital and are not mutual sureties for each other.
قبض (qabd): Possession; physical taking of the gifted property, a condition for the gift to transfer ownership.
عرض ('ard): Goods other than gold and silver (cash equivalents). In this context, goods that are not valid for mufawadah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a mufawadah partnership become invalid and turn into an 'inan partnership due to inheritance?
A) When either partner inherits any property, regardless of type.
B) When one partner inherits goods that are not cash equivalents.
C) When one partner inherits cash or its equivalent, because it increases his capital and breaks equality.
D) Only if the inheritance is accompanied by a formal contract of conversion.

Key Takeaway
A mufawadah partnership converts to 'inan only when a partner acquires additional cash-like property (with possession if gifted); goods and real estate have no effect.

Definition and Features of 'Inan Partnership
Defining 'inan partnership and its core features

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of 'inan partnership and identify its three core features: no suretyship, agency included, and capital inequality permitted.

Core Idea
'Inan partnership: shared trade, no mutual debt liability, mutual agency, and unequal capital allowed.

In Hanafi fiqh, partnerships are classified into four types; the most common is 'inan. This passage defines 'inan and highlights its key characteristics, distinguishing it from mufawadah. Understanding these features is essential for structuring a valid partnership contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the partnership type
Recognize that 'inan is the second type of shirkah mentioned in the text, after mufawadah.
The text lists four types; 'inan is defined first.
2
Extract the core definition
Note that 'inan is described as a partnership in all kinds of trade or a specific type, such as cloth or food.
This flexibility distinguishes it from partnerships limited to a single venture.
3
Analyze inclusions and exclusions
The text explicitly states that 'inan does not include suretyship (kafalah) but does include agency (wakalah).
This is a crucial difference from mufawadah, which includes both.
4
Check capital requirements
The passage clarifies that equality of capital is not required; the partnership is valid even if one partner contributes more or only part of their wealth.
This contrasts with mufawadah where equal capital is a condition.
5
Contrast with mufawadah
Mufawadah (first type) requires equality of capital, full mutual agency, and mutual suretyship. 'inan lacks the last two elements.
Memorize these distinctions for future fatwa work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعنان : وهو شركة في كلّ تجارة، أو في نوع، ولا تتضمن الكفالة، وتصح ببعض ماله [٤] قوله : وعنان ; عطف على قوله : مفاوضة ; أي أحدها : مفاوضة ، وثانيها : عنان، وثالثها: شركة الصنائع ، ورابعها شركة الوجوه، وسيأتي ذكرهما، والعنان بكسر العين المهملة، وجاء فتحها أيضا ، يقال : عن له وكذا عناناً : أي ظهر له أن يشاركه في بعض ماله. [٥]قوله : وهو ; يعني العنان عبارة عن شركة اثنين فما فوقها في جميع أنواع التجارات أو في نوع منهما كالتجارة في البزّ ، أو في الطعام، أو في غير ذلك. [٦] قوله : ولا تتضمن ; أي لا تتضمن شركة عنان الكفالة، فلا يكون أحدهما كفيلاً عن الآخر فيما لزمه، وتتضمّنُ الوكالة ; ليتحقق مقصود الشركة. [۷] قوله : وتصح ببعض ماله ; يعني لا يشترط في شركة العنان تساوي المالين ، فتصح ببعض المال وبكله مع زيادة مالِ أحدهما ، وبتساوي المالين من دون تساوي.
And 'inan: it is a partnership in every trade, or in a type, and it does not include suretyship, and it is valid with some of one's wealth. [4] His statement: 'And 'inan' — it is a conjunction to his statement: 'mufawadah', i.e., one of them is mufawadah, the second is 'inan, the third is partnership of craftsmen, and the fourth is partnership of faces, and they will be mentioned later. And 'inan is with kasra on the 'ayn, and it is also said with fatha. It is said: 'anna lahu' and also 'inan', i.e., it appeared to him to partner him in some of his property. [5] His statement: 'it is' — meaning 'inan is a partnership of two or more in all kinds of trades or in one type, such as trade in cloth, or food, or other than that. [6] His statement: 'and it does not include' — i.e., the 'inan partnership does not include suretyship, so one of them is not a surety for the other for what becomes binding on him, and it does include agency (wakalah) to achieve the purpose of partnership. [7] His statement: 'and it is valid with some of his wealth' — meaning that equality of the two capitals is not required in 'inan partnership, so it is valid with part of the wealth and with all of it with an increase in one partner's capital, and with equality of the two capitals without equality (in other aspects?).
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of partnership: 'inan (from the root meaning 'to appear'). It is a partnership in which two or more persons agree to engage in all kinds of trade or a specific type (e.g., cloth, food). Key features: (1) It does not entail mutual suretyship (kafalah), meaning partners are not automatically liable for each other's personal debts; it does entail mutual agency (wakalah) to conduct the partnership business. (2) The capital contributed does not have to be equal; one partner may contribute more than the other, and the partnership is valid even if only a portion of one's wealth is invested. The commentary also lists the four types of partnership in Hanafi law: mufawadah, 'inan, partnership of craftsmen (sina'i'), and partnership of faces (wujuh).

Key Terms
عنان ('inan): A partnership in which partners invest in a specific trade or all trades, without mutual suretyship, and with possible inequality of capital.
وكالة (wakalah): Agency; the legal relationship where each partner is authorized to act on behalf of the other in partnership matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a characteristic of 'inan partnership according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) It can be in all types of trade or a specific type.
B) It includes mutual suretyship between partners.
C) It is valid even if one partner invests more capital.
D) It includes mutual agency for partnership transactions.

Key Takeaway
'Inan partnership is defined by shared investment in trade, mutual agency, absence of suretyship, and allowance for unequal capital.

Unequal Profit in 'Inan Partnership: Hanafi Position
Defines 'inan partnership and presents Hanafi view allowing unequal profit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits unequal profit in 'inan partnership even with equal capital, and evaluate the counter-argument of al-Shafi'i and Zufar.

Core Idea
In 'inan partnership, profit may be earned by work, so unequal shares are valid even with equal capital.

The 'inan partnership is a flexible contract that does not require surety. A key dispute arises when partners contribute equal capital but wish to distribute profit unevenly. This lesson examines the Hanafi justification based on mudarabah analogy and the opposing view requiring proportionality.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling on profit distribution in 'inan partnership when capital is equal.
Hanafi: unequal profit is valid by agreement.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Name the scholars who require proportionality and state their position.
Zufar (Hanafi) and al-Shafi'i: profit must match capital ratio.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Explain the reasoning behind the opposing view, including the principle 'al-kharaj bi al-daman'.
Profit follows guarantee; unequal profit would mean profit without liability.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Explain the Hanafi rebuttal using analogy to mudarabah and istihsan.
Profit can be earned through work, as in mudarabah; partners may have unequal skills.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical difference in rulings between the two positions.
Hanafi: 50-50 capital, 70-30 profit valid. Shafi'i: must be 50-50 profit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعنان: وهو شركة في كل تجارة، أو في نوع، ولا تتضمن الكفالة، وتصح ببعض ماله، ومع فضل مال أحدهما، وتساوي ماليها لا الربح: أي يصح بأن يشترط أن يكون المال مساوياً، ولا يكون الربح مساوياً وبالعكس خلافاً لزفر والشافعي. فإن عندهما يشترط التساوي في الربح مع تساوي المال؛ لأن التفاضل في الربح يؤدي إلى ربح ما لم يضمن، فإن المال إذا كان نصفين، والربح أثلاثاً فصاحب الزيادة يستحقها بلا ضمان، إذ الضمان بقدر رأس المال؛ ولذا لا تكون الوضيعة إلا على قدر المالين. ولا يصح اشتراط الخسران الزائد على أحدهما اتفاقاً، وأيضاً الشركة في الربح شركة في الأصل، فربح المال بمنزلة نماء الأعيان، فيستحق بقدر الملك في الأصل. ونحن نقول: الربح كما يستحق بالمال كذلك قد يستحق بالعمل فقط، أيضاً كما في عقد المضاربة؛ فإن المال فيه يكون لأحداهما، ومن الآخر العمل، والربح يكون مشتركاً. ومن المعلوم أن أحد الشريكين قد يكون أحذق وأهدى وأكثر عملاً فلا يرضى بمساواة الربح، فلا بد أن يجوز التفاضل. والسر فيه: أن عقد العنان يشبه المضاربة من حيث أنه يعمل في مال الشريك، ويشبه المفاوضة اسماً وعملاً، فعملنا يشبه المضاربة بجواز اشتراط ربح ما لم يضمن، وعملنا يشبه المفاوضة، فقلنا بعدم بطلانه باشتراط العمل عليهما.
And 'inan: it is a partnership in every trade, or in a type, and it does not include surety (kafala), and it is valid with some of one's wealth, and with a surplus of the wealth of one of them, and equality of the two capitals but not the profit: meaning it is valid that they stipulate that the capital be equal and the profit not be equal, and vice versa, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i. For according to them, equality in profit is required with equality of capital, because disparity in profit leads to profit from what one did not guarantee (daman). For if the capital is halves and the profit is thirds, then the one with the extra claims it without guarantee, since guarantee is according to the capital. Therefore, loss is only according to the proportion of the two capitals. And it is not valid to stipulate excess loss on one of them by agreement. Also, partnership in profit is partnership in the asset, so profit of the capital is like growth of the corpus, so it is deserved according to ownership in the asset. And we say: profit, just as it is deserved by capital, so too it may be deserved by work alone, as in a mudarabah contract. For in that contract, the capital belongs to one party, work to the other, and the profit is shared. And it is known that one of the two partners may be more skilled, more prudent, and more hardworking, so he would not be satisfied with equality of profit, so disparity must be permitted. And the secret of it is that the 'inan contract resembles mudarabah in that one works with the partner's capital, and it resembles mufawada in name and in operation. So our opinion resembles mudarabah in permitting stipulation of profit from what one did not guarantee, and our opinion resembles mufawada, so we say it is not invalidated by stipulating work on both parties.
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the 'inan partnership. The Hanafi position is that in an 'inan contract, the partners may agree to unequal profit shares even if their capital contributions are equal, and vice versa. The opposing view, held by Imam Zufar (a Hanafi scholar who differed on this) and Imam al-Shafi'i, requires that profit be proportional to capital when capital is equal. Their argument is based on the principle that profit follows liability (al-kharaj bi al-daman): if one partner receives more profit than his capital share, he profits from what he did not guarantee (daman). Additionally, they analogize profit to the natural increase of an owned asset, which belongs to the owner in proportion to his ownership. The Hanafi rebuttal, grounded in istihsan, is that profit can be earned through work as well as capital, analogous to the mudarabah contract where the capital owner and the worker share profit. Since partners may contribute unequal effort, skill, or labor, it is reasonable to allow unequal profit distribution. The Hanafi view also draws on the dual nature of 'inan: it resembles mudarabah in that each partner may work with the other's capital, and it resembles mufawada in name and practice, thus allowing flexibility. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the Hanafi view permitting disparity in profit.

Key Terms
عنان (‘inan): A partnership where partners contribute capital and labor optionally, and it does not include mutual surety (kafala). It can be in all trades or a specific trade.
مفاوضة (mufawada): A full partnership where partners are equal in capital, profit, and liability, and each is a surety for the other.
مضاربة (mudarabah): A contract where one party provides capital and the other provides labor, and profit is shared according to agreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a valid Hanafi ‘inan partnership, if both partners contribute equal capital, what is the ruling on profit distribution?
A) Profit must be equal because profit follows capital.
B) Profit may be unequal if both agree, based on analogy to mudarabah.
C) Profit must be equal only if the partners are adults.
D) Profit cannot exceed the capital contribution.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi 'inan partnership, profit disparity is allowed because profit may be earned by work, not solely by capital.

Profit Disparity in Shirkah al-'Inan: Hanafi Permissibility
Ruling on profit disparity with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on unequal profit shares in shirkah al-'inan and evaluate the contrary arguments of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Profit in partnership can be earned through work, not just capital, justifying unequal shares.

This lesson examines a key dispute in partnership law: can partners agree to split profit unevenly even when their capital contributions are equal? The Hanafi school says yes, basing this on the nature of work in the partnership; Zufar and al-Shafi'i say no, insisting that profit must follow liability. Through this passage, you will learn the reasoning on both sides and the practical consequences of the disagreement.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling from the matn: it is valid for partners to stipulate equal capital but unequal profit, and vice versa. Note that stipulating all profit for one partner invalidates the contract by consensus.
The rule allows flexibility based on work contribution.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar and al-Shafi'i require equality of profit when capital is equal. Understand their core argument: disparity leads to 'ribh ma lam yadman' (profit without liability).
Opponents ground their view in the principle 'al-kharaj bi al-daman'.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine their dalil: profit is like natural growth of an asset, so it belongs proportionally to ownership. Loss is also proportional, and partnership in profit implies partnership in principal.
They analogize profit to the growth of a jointly owned animal or tree.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal: profit can be earned through work as well as capital, as in mudarabah. Also, partners may differ in skill and effort, so inequality is practically necessary.
The secret (sirr) is that shirkah al-'inan resembles both mudarabah and mufawadah.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafi allows unequal profit shares; Shafi'i requires equality if capital is equal. This affects how partnership contracts are drafted in practice.
The Hanafi view is mufta bihi, as stated in al-Hidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومع فضل مال أحدهما، وتساوي ماليها لا الربح) : أي يصح بأن يشترط أن يكون المال مساوياً ، ولا يكون الربح مساوياً وبالعكس خلافاً لزفر والشافعي ۱۲ (۱) الربح، وكذا عكسه، وتصح من أهلِ التَّوكيل ، وإن لم يكن من أهل الكفالة كصبي ومعتوه يعقل البيع. [1] قوله : ولا يكون ؛ وأمَّا اشتراط جميع الربح لأحدهما فلا يجوز اتفاقاً ؛ لأنه عند يخرج به العقد من الشركة، كذا في «الهداية»(۳). [٢] قوله : خلافا لزفر والشافعي الله ؛ فإنَّ عندهما يشترط التّساوي في الربح . تساوي المال ؛ لأن التفاضل في الربح يؤدّي إلى ربح ما لم يضمن، فإن المال إذا كان نصفين، والربح أثلاثاً فصاحبُ الزّيادة يستحقها بلا ضمان، إذ الضمان بقدرِ رأس المال ؛ ولذا لا تكون الوضيعة إلا على قدر المالين. ولا يصح اشتراط الخسرانِ الزَّائدِ على أحدهما اتفاقاً، وأيضاً الشركة في الربح شركة في الأصل ، فربح المال بمنزلة نماء الأعيان، فيستحق بقدر الملك في الأصل. ونحن نقول : الرِّبحُ كَما يستحق بالمال كذلك قد يستحق بالعمل فقط ، أيضاً كما في عقد المضاربة ؛ فإنَّ المال فيه يكون لأحداهما، ومن الآخر العمل، والربح يكون مشتركاً. ومن المعلوم أن أحد الشريكين قد يكون أحذق وأهدى وأكثر عملاً فلا يرضى بمساواة الربح، فلا بد أن يجوز التفاضل. والسر فيه : أن عقد العنان يشبه المضاربة من حيث أنه يعمل في عمل الشريك، ويشبه المفاوضة اسماً وعملاً، فعملنا يشبه المضاربة من حيث أنه يعمل في مـال الشريك، ويشبه المفاوضة اسماً وعملاً ، فعملنا يشبه المضاربة بجواز اشتراط ربح ما لم يضمن، وعملنا يشبه المفاوضة ، فقلنا بعدم بطلانه باشتراط العمل عليهما، كذا في «الهداية»(۳).
And with preference of the capital of one of them, and equality of their capitals not the profit: That is, it is valid by stipulating that the capital be equal, and the profit not be equal, and vice versa, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i. Profit, and likewise its opposite. And it is valid from people of agency, even if not from people of suretyship, like a boy and an insane person who understand sale. [1] His saying: and it is not; As for stipulating all the profit for one of them, it is not permissible by consensus, because it removes the contract from partnership, thus in al-Hidayah. [2] His saying: contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i; For according to them, equality in profit is stipulated when capital is equal, because disparity in profit leads to profit for what one did not guarantee. If the capital is halves and the profit is thirds, the owner of the increase claims it without guarantee, since guarantee is proportional to capital. Hence loss is only proportional to the two capitals. Stipulating excess loss on one of them is not valid by consensus. Also, partnership in profit is partnership in the principal, so profit of capital is like growth of assets, so it is deserved according to ownership in principal. And we say: Profit, just as it is deserved by capital, so too it may be deserved by work alone, as in the contract of mudarabah; for capital therein belongs to one party, and work from the other, and profit is shared. And it is known that one of the partners may be more skilled, more guided, and more working, so he would not be satisfied with equality of profit, so disparity must be permissible. And the secret therein: The contract of 'inan resembles mudarabah in that he works in the capital of the partner, and resembles mufawadah in name and work. So our work resembles mudarabah in permissibility of stipulating profit for what is not guaranteed, and our work resembles mufawadah, so we said it is not invalidated by stipulating work upon both, thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding profit distribution in a shirkah al-'inan (limited partnership) contract. The matn (base text) states the ruling: it is valid for partners to agree on unequal profit shares even if their capital contributions are equal, and vice versa. The commentator explains that this is permissible only if both partners work; stipulating all profit for one partner invalidates the contract by consensus. The dispute: Zufar and al-Shafi'i require equality of profit when capital is equal, arguing that disparity leads to a partner earning profit without liability (since liability is proportional to capital). They analogize profit to natural growth of an asset, which follows ownership. The Hanafi response: profit can be earned through work as well as capital, as in mudarabah (investment partnership). Since one partner may contribute more skill or labor, inequality is necessary. The deeper reason: shirkah al-'inan combines elements of mudarabah (work in partner's capital) and mufawadah (full partnership), so it permits stipulating profit not guaranteed by capital. This is the relied-upon Hanafi position from al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
شركة العنان (shirkah al-'inan): A partnership where each partner contributes capital and work, but they are not obligatory agents for each other beyond specified matters; it is a limited partnership.
ربح ما لم يضمن (ribh ma lam yadman): Profit earned on capital for which one has no liability; in this context, it refers to a partner receiving a share of profit disproportionate to his capital contribution, which opponents argue is unjust.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is it valid for partners in shirkah al-'inan to agree that one partner receives a larger share of profit even though both contributed equal capital?
A) Yes, because profit can be earned through work as well as capital.
B) No, because profit must be proportional to capital contribution.
C) Yes, but only if both partners waive their right to profit.
D) No, because it would lead to a loss of liability.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi shirkah al-'inan, unequal profit shares are valid because profit can be generated by work, not just capital; the contract is void only if all profit goes to one partner.

Shirkah — Partnership
intermediate
8 min
Permissibility of Different Capitals in Shirkah al-'Inan
Ruling with proof on validity of partnership with different capitals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on shirkah al-'inan when partners contribute different currencies and explain the proof based on contract versus capital.

Core Idea
Partnership in profit is based on contract, not mixing capital – so different currencies are valid.

This passage addresses a key dispute: can two partners in shirkah al-'inan invest in different types of currency? The Hanafi school allows it, while Zufar and al-Shafi'i require mixing and unity of type. Understanding the proof clarifies why the contract itself is the foundation of partnership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The matn states that shirkah al-'inan is valid even when one partner contributes dirhams and the other dinars, and without mixing the capitals. The ruling is 'sahih' (valid).
2
Locate the opposing views
Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold that such a partnership is invalid because they require mixing of capitals and unity of type (e.g., both in dirhams).
3
Analyze the opposing proof
Their evidence: profit is a branch (far') of capital; partnership in profit requires partnership in the principal (asl), which is only achieved by physical mixing. Different currencies cannot be mixed, so partnership fails.
They also derive from this that profit shares must be proportional to capital.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Hanafi position: partnership in profit is based on the contract (aqd), not on the capital. Therefore, mixing or unity of type is irrelevant. The contract itself creates the partnership in profit.
This is a key aqli proof rooted in the nature of the contract.
5
Determine the practical difference
Hanafi permits partnerships with different currencies without mixing; the opposing school requires same currency and physical mixing. This affects real-world business arrangements.
The Hanafi ruling is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكون مال أحدهما دراهم، والآخر دنانير، وبلا خلط ، خلافاً لزفر والشافعي (1) . (وكل مطالب بثمن مشريه لا غير : أي لا غير المشترى بناءً على أنه لا يتضمن الكفالة [١] قوله : وكـون ... الخ ؛ يعني تجوز شركة العنانِ مع اختلاف مالي الشريكين جنساً كان يكون مال أحدهما دراهم، ومالُ الآخر دنانير، وكذا مع اختلافهما وصفاً كأن يكون من أحدهما دراهم بيض، ومن الآخر سود. وعند زفر والشافعي لا يجوز ذلك، وهذا الخلاف مبني على الخلاف في الخلط ، فإنَّ عندهما خلط المالين شرط ، ولا يتحقق ذلك في المالين المختلفين. وعندنا : تجوز الشركة وإن لم يخلط المال، وإذا لم يكن الخلط شرطاً لم يضرّ اختلاف الجنس والوصف. لهما في اشتراط الخلط : أن الربح فرع المال، ولا يقعُ الفرعُ على الشركة إلا بعد الشركة في الأصل، والشركة إنما تكون بالخلط، وعلى هذا الأصل يتفرع عندهما : اتحاد الجنس وعدم جواز التفاضلِ في الربح مع التّساوي في المال، وعدم جواز شركة التقبل على ما سيأتي. ونحن نقول : إنَّ الشَّركة في الربح مستندة إلى العقد دون المال، فلم يكن الخلط شرطاً، ولا اتحاد الجنس، ولا بتساوي الربح ، كذا في «الهداية) وحواشيها (۳). (٢) قوله : وكل ؛ أي كل واحدٍ من شريكي العنان يطالب بثمن ما اشتراه لا الشريك الآخر؛ لما ذكرنا أنها تتضمَّنُ الوكالة دون الكفالة، والوكيل هو المطالب في حقوق العقار.
And that the capital of one is dirhams, and the other dinars, and without mixing, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i. (And each is demanded for the price of his purchase only, i.e., not beyond what was purchased, based on the fact that it does not include suretyship. [1] His saying: And that... etc.; He means that shirkah al-'inan is permissible with difference in the two partners' capitals in type, e.g., one's capital is dirhams and the other's dinars, and likewise with difference in description, e.g., one has white dirhams and the other black. According to Zufar and al-Shafi'i, that is not permissible. This dispute is based on the dispute on mixing; for according to them, mixing the two capitals is a condition, and it is not realized in two different capitals. According to us: the partnership is permissible even if the capital is not mixed, and if mixing is not a condition, difference in type and description does not harm. Their proof for stipulating mixing: profit is a branch of capital, and the branch cannot be based on partnership except after partnership in the principal, and partnership is only by mixing. Upon this principle follow, according to them, unity of type, impermissibility of disparity in profit when capital is equal, and impermissibility of shirkah al-taqabbul as will come. And we say: Partnership in profit is based on the contract, not on the capital, so mixing is not a condition, nor unity of type, nor equality of profit, thus in al-Hidayah and its glosses. (2) His saying: and each; i.e., each one of the two partners in shirkah al-'inan is demanded for the price of what he bought, not the other partner; because it (the contract) includes agency but not suretyship, and the agent is the one demanded in rights of immovable property.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding two issues in shirkah al-'inan: (1) The validity of the partnership when the capitals are of different types (e.g., dirhams and dinars) or different descriptions (e.g., white and black coins), and (2) the liability of each partner for purchases made. The matn states that the partnership is valid even with different capital types and without mixing, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i. The commentator explains the basis of the dispute: Zufar and al-Shafi'i require mixing of capital for a valid partnership, so different currencies cannot be mixed and thus the partnership is invalid. They analogize profit to a branch of capital, requiring unity of the underlying asset. The Hanafi position, from al-Hidayah, is that partnership in profit is based on the contract, not on the capital, so mixing is not required. Consequently, differences in type or description are irrelevant. The second ruling: each partner in shirkah al-'inan is individually liable for the price of what he himself purchased, not the other partner. This is because the contract includes agency (wakalah) but not suretyship (kafalah). The agent alone is responsible for transactions he enters.

Key Terms
خلط (khalṭ): Mixing of the two partners' capital together so that they become indistinguishable; according to some schools, it is a condition for establishing partnership.
شركة العنان (shirkah al-'inan): A partnership where each partner contributes capital and authorizes the other to trade, sharing profits according to agreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the basis of partnership in profit in shirkah al-'inan?
A) The physical mixing of capital
B) The contract ('aqd) between the partners
C) The similarity of capital types
D) The unity of currency

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates shirkah al-'inan with different capitals because the partnership is based on the contract, not on physical mixing of assets.

Shirkah — Partnership

Individual Liability in Shirkah al-'Inan
Ruling on individual liability in shirkah al-'inan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why each partner in shirkah al-'inan is liable only for his own purchases and apply the distinction between agency and suretyship.

Core Idea
In shirkah al-'inan, each partner is an agent for joint profit but not a surety for the other's debts.

This passage clarifies a practical consequence of the nature of shirkah al-'inan: each partner is responsible only for what he himself buys. This follows from the fact that the contract includes agency (wakalah) but not suretyship (kafalah). Understanding this protects partners from unexpected liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The matn states: 'Each one of the two partners in shirkah al-'inan is demanded for the price of what he bought, not the other partner.' This means each partner is individually liable for his own purchases.
2
Understand the basis
The commentator explains that this is because shirkah al-'inan includes mutual agency (wakalah) but not suretyship (kafalah). The agent is responsible for transactions he enters into.
Wakalah allows each partner to trade on behalf of the partnership, but kafalah would make one liable for the other's debts.
3
Apply to a scenario
If partner A buys goods on credit from a seller, only A is liable to pay. The seller cannot demand payment from partner B.
This is true even if the goods are for the partnership business.
4
Compare with other forms
In contrast, in shirkah al-mufawadah (unlimited partnership), partners may be jointly liable. In shirkah al-'inan, liability is individual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding two issues in shirkah al-'inan: (1) The validity of the partnership when the capitals are of different types (e.g., dirhams and dinars) or different descriptions (e.g., white and black coins), and (2) the liability of each partner for purchases made. This is because the contract includes agency (wakalah) but not suretyship (kafalah). The agent alone is responsible for transactions he enters.

Key Terms
وكالة (wakālah): Agency; in shirkah al-'inan, each partner is an agent for the other only in transactions related to the partnership property, but not a surety.
كفالة (kafālah): Suretyship; a contract where one person guarantees the debt or obligation of another. Not included in shirkah al-'inan.

In Hanafi shirkah al-'inan, why is each partner not liable for the other's purchases?
A) Because the contract includes suretyship (kafalah).
B) Because the contract includes agency (wakalah) but not suretyship.
C) Because the partnership is invalid.
D) Because the purchases are personal, not for the partnership.

Key Takeaway
In shirkah al-'inan, each partner bears sole liability for his own transactions because the contract provides agency, not suretyship.

Shirkah — Partnership
intermediate

Conditions of Shirkat al-ʿInān: Mixing, Currency, and Liability
Inter-madhhab dispute on mixing and currency, plus liability rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that shirkat al-ʿinān is valid without mixing capital and with different currencies, and explain why each partner is only liable for his own purchases.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, shirkat al-ʿinān is valid even without mixing capital and with different currencies; liability is individual, not joint.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqāyah addresses two key conditions for shirkat al-ʿinān (limited partnership). First, the Hanafī school diverges from Zufar and al-Shāfiʿī on whether mixing the capitals (khalṭ) and uniformity of currency (jins) are prerequisites for validity. Second, it clarifies that each partner is only responsible for the price of what he personally buys, because the partnership includes agency (wakālah) but not guarantee (kafālah). These rules shape the practical operation of Hanafī partnerships.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Pinpoint the two distinct rulings in the passage: (1) validity of partnership with different currencies and without mixing, and (2) each partner is liable only for his own purchases.
The second ruling is stated concisely: 'وكل مطالب بثمن مشريه لا غير'.
2
State the Hanafī position on mixing and currency
The Hanafī school holds that mixing is not a condition; partnership is valid even if one partner's capital is dirhams and the other's is dinars, and without physical mixing.
This is the majority view (Abū Ḥanīfa, Abū Yūsuf, Muḥammad).
3
Analyze the opposing view and its basis
Zufar and al-Shāfiʿī require mixing and same currency, arguing that profit is a branch of capital, so partnership in profit requires partnership in the capital itself, achieved only through mixing.
Their principle leads to further rules: unity of type, no disparity in profit with equal capital, and invalidity of shirkat al-taqabbul.
4
Examine the Hanafī response
The Hanafī school counters that partnership in profit is based on the contract (al-ʿaqd), not on the capital. Therefore, mixing and currency uniformity are irrelevant.
This is stated in al-Hidāyah: 'الشركة في الربح مستندة إلى العقد دون المال'.
5
Apply the liability rule
Each partner is only liable for his own purchases because the contract entails agency (not guarantee). The seller can only demand payment from the actual buyer.
This follows from the principle that shirkat al-ʿinān includes wakālah but not kafālah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشركة أنواعها وأحكامها
وكون مال أحدهما دراهم، والآخر دنانير، وبلا خلط ، وكل مطالب بثمن مشريه لا غير
[1] قوله : وكـون ... الخ؛ يعني تجوز شركة العنانِ مع اختلاف مالي الشريكين جنساً كان يكون مال أحدهما دراهم، ومالُ الآخر دنانير، وكذا مع اختلافهما وصفاً كأن يكون من أحدهما دراهم بيض، ومن الآخر سود. وعند زفر والشافعي لا يجوز ذلك، وهذا الخلاف مبني على الخلاف في الخلط ، فإنَّ عندهما خلط المالين شرط ، ولا يتحقق ذلك في المالين المختلفين. وعندنا : تجوز الشركة وإن لم يخلط المال، وإذا لم يكن الخلط شرطاً لم يضرّ
اختلاف الجنس والوصف.
لهما في اشتراط الخلط : أن الربح فرع المال، ولا يقعُ الفرعُ على الشركة إلا بعد الشركة في الأصل، والشركة إنما تكون بالخلط، وعلى هذا الأصل يتفرع عندهما : اتحاد الجنس وعدم جواز التفاضلِ في الربح مع التّساوي في المال، وعدم جواز شركة التقبل على ما سيأتي.
ونحن نقول : إنَّ الشَّركة في الربح مستندة إلى العقد دون المال، فلم يكن الخلط شرطاً، ولا اتحاد الجنس، ولا بتساوي الربح ، كذا في «الهداية) وحواشيها (۳). (٢) قوله : وكل ؛ أي كل واحدٍ من شريكي العنان يطالب بثمن ما اشتراه لا الشريك الآخر؛ لما ذكرنا أنها تتضمَّنُ الوكالة دون الكفالة، والوكيل هو المطالب في
حقوق العقار.
The Book of Partnership: Its Types and Rulings
And that the property of one [partner] is dirhams and of the other is dinars, and without mixing, and each is demanded only for the price of his purchase.
[1] His statement: 'And that...' etc.; meaning that 'inan partnership is permissible with difference in the capital of the two partners in type, such that one partner's capital is dirhams and the other's is dinars, and similarly with difference in description, such as one having white dirhams and the other black. According to Zufar and al-Shafi'i, this is not permissible, and this difference is built upon the difference regarding mixing. For in their view, mixing of the two capitals is a condition, and that is not realized with two different capitals. According to us: partnership is permissible even if the capital is not mixed, and when mixing is not a condition, difference in type and description does not harm.
For them [Zufar and al-Shafi'i] in requiring mixing: that profit is a branch of capital, and the branch cannot occur on partnership except after partnership in the origin, and partnership is only through mixing. And upon this principle branches for them: unity of type and impermissibility of disparity in profit with equality in capital, and impermissibility of partnership of acceptance (shirkat al-taqabbul) as will come.
And we say: partnership in profit is based on the contract, not on the capital, so neither mixing, nor unity of type, nor equality of profit is a condition, as in al-Hidayah and its glosses (3). (2) His statement: 'and each'; i.e., each one of the two partners of 'inan partnership is demanded for the price of what he bought, not the other partner; because we mentioned that it includes agency without guarantee, and the agent is the one demanded in rights of real property.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqaya explains two conditions for the validity of شركة العنان (partnership with limited liability). First, the Hanafi school holds that it is permissible for the two partners to contribute capital of different types (e.g., one in dirhams, the other in dinars) and even without physically mixing the capitals. This is contrary to the view of Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) and Imam al-Shafi'i, who require that the capitals be of the same type and mixed. The dispute stems from a fundamental difference: Zufar and al-Shafi'i view the sharing of profit as dependent on sharing in the capital itself, which is achieved through mixing. The Hanafi school, however, holds that the partnership in profit is based on the contract, not on the capital. Therefore, mixing is not a condition. Second, each partner is only liable for the price of what he personally purchases, not for his partner's purchases, because the partnership contract entails agency (الوكالة) but not guarantee (الكفالة). As a result, the seller can only demand payment from the actual buyer.

Key Terms
خلط (khalṭ): The physical mixing of the two partners' capitals.
شركة العنان (shirkat al-ʿinān): A partnership where each partner contributes capital and has the right to trade, with profit and loss according to their capital.
كفالة (kafālah): Guarantee; being liable for another's obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafī school, which of the following is correct regarding shirkat al-ʿinān?
A) The partnership is invalid if the capitals are of different types.
B) The partnership is invalid without physical mixing of capitals.
C) Each partner is only liable for his own purchases.
D) Both partners are jointly liable for all purchases.

Key Takeaway
Shirkat al-ʿinān is valid without mixing capital and with different currencies; each partner is solely liable for his own contracts.
