Shirkat al-Wujuh: Validity and Conditions in Hanafi Law
Defines shirkat al-wujuh and defends its validity as mufawada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on shirkat al-wujuh, explain the basis for its validity, and identify the conditions required for it to be valid as mufawada.

Core Idea
Profit in partnership can be based on guarantee (daman), not only on capital — Hanafi validation of credit-based partnerships.

In this lesson, we examine a distinctive Hanafi partnership called shirkat al-wujuh, where two individuals without capital buy goods on credit using their social standing, then sell and share the profit. This partnership is rejected by Imam al-Shafi'i on the grounds that profit must derive from capital. The Hanafi school, however, validates it as a form of mufawada partnership, grounding the profit entitlement in the contract and the guarantee (daman) assumed by the partners. We will explore the definition, the inter-madhhab dispute, and the specific conditions that must be met for validity.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
State the ruling and key elements of shirkat al-wujuh: two partners without capital purchase goods on credit using their reputation, sell, pay the seller, and share the remaining profit. The Hanafi school declares this valid as mufawada.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Identify Imam al-Shafi'i as the opponent. His view: the partnership is invalid because profit (ribh) is a branch (far') of capital (mal). Without capital, there can be no profit and no valid partnership.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i proof is based on the principle that profit is generated from capital. Since shirkat al-wujuh lacks capital investment, there is no basis for profit.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: partnership in profit is based on the contract ('aqd), not solely on capital. The guarantee (daman) undertaken by the partners to pay the price serves as the basis for profit. This is a valid foundation for a partnership contract.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In Hanafi law, shirkat al-wujuh is a valid, binding partnership. In Shafi'i law, it is void (batil). This affects how scholars classify such arrangements in trade and finance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
4. وشركة الوجوه : وهي أن يشتركا بلا مال ليشتريا بوجههما ويبيعا أي ليشتريا بلا نقدِ الثّمن ؛ بسبب وجاهتهما ، فما حصل من الثَّمن يدفعان منه الثمن إلى بائعهما ، فإن فضل منه شيء يكون مشتركاً بينهما، وهذه الشركة لا تجوز" عند الشَّافِعِي . (فتصح مفاوضة) : بأن يشترط المساواة في الأمور التي يجب مساواتها في المفاوضة. [قوله : لا يجوز؛ لأن الربح عنده فرعُ المال، فإذا لم يوجد لا تنعقد شركة، وعندنا : الشركة في الربح مستندة إلى العقد على ما مرّ. قوله : فتصح ؛ أي شركة الوجوه ؛ وكذا شركة التقبل تصح مفاوضة ، فصورة اجتماع شرائط المفاوضة في التَّقبل أن يشتركَ الصَّانعان على أن يتقبلا جمعياً الأعمال، وأن يضمنا العمل جميعاً على التساوي، وأن يتساويا في الربح والوضيعة، وأن يكون كل منها كفيلاً عن الآخر فيما لحقه بالشركة. وصورة المفاوضة في شركة الوجوه : أن يكون الرّجلان من أهل الكفالة، ويكون ثمن المشترى بينهما نصفين، وأن يتلفّظا بلفظ المفاوضة ، ويتساويا في الربح. كذا في «المحيط» و «النهاية» و«الفتح».
4. And partnership of faces (wujuh): It is that two persons enter into partnership without capital in order to buy with their reputation (faces) and sell, meaning to buy without paying the price, due to their prestige. Then whatever they obtain from the price, they pay the price from it to their seller. If anything remains, it is shared between them. This partnership is not valid according to al-Shafi'i. (It is valid as mufawada) by stipulating equality in matters in which equality is required in mufawada. [Comment: He said: It is not valid because profit according to him is a branch of capital, so if capital does not exist, the partnership does not contract. According to us: Partnership in profit is based on the contract as previously mentioned. He said: It is valid, i.e., shirkat al-wujuh; likewise shirkat al-taqabbul is valid as mufawada. The form of meeting the conditions of mufawada in taqabbul is that two craftsmen partner to collectively accept work, guarantee the work equally, be equal in profit and loss, and each be a guarantor for the other in what pertains to the partnership. And the form of mufawada in shirkat al-wujuh is that the two men are among those eligible for kafala (guarantee), the price of the purchase is half and half between them, they utter the term mufawada, and are equal in profit. Thus in al-Muhit, al-Nihaya, and al-Fath.]
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage defines and validates shirkat al-wujuh (partnership based on credit/reputation) according to the Hanafi school. It is an inter-madhhab dispute because Imam al-Shafi'i invalidates it. The passage first defines the partnership: two persons without capital buy on credit using their reputation, then sell and pay the seller from the proceeds; the remainder is shared. The Shafi'i position is that profit is a branch of capital, so without capital no partnership can exist. The Hanafi response, stated concisely, is that partnership in profit is based on the contract ('aqd) itself, not necessarily on capital. The commentary then explains that shirkat al-wujuh is valid as a mufawada partnership, provided the conditions of mufawada are met: equality in capital contribution, profit, and liability. The conditions for shirkat al-taqabbul (partnership in accepting work) are also given: both undertake the work jointly, guarantee performance equally, share profit and loss equally, and each is guarantor for the other. For shirkat al-wujuh specifically, the partners must be eligible for kafala (guarantee), the purchased goods must be owned equally (half each), they must explicitly use the term 'mufawada', and profits must be equal. The authorities cited are al-Muhit, al-Nihaya, and al-Fath.

Key Terms
شركة الوجوه (shirkat al-wujuh): A partnership where two persons without capital buy on credit using their reputation and social standing, then sell and share the profit after paying the price.
مفاوضة (mufawada): A type of partnership with complete equality in capital, profit, and liability, where each partner is agent and guarantor for the other.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee; in shirkat al-wujuh, the partners guarantee the payment for purchased goods, which serves as the basis for profit entitlement.
شركة التقبل (shirkat al-taqabbul): Partnership in accepting work, where two craftsmen jointly undertake a job and share the fee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Labor only
C) Guarantee (daman)
D) Social status alone

Key Takeaway
Shirkat al-wujuh is a valid mufawada partnership in Hanafi law, with profit based on the contract and guarantee, not on capital.

Profit Proportionality in Partnerships
Ruling on profit division with talil (reasoning)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that profit shares must correspond to ownership shares in purchased goods in absolute partnerships, and identify the exceptions for mudaraba and wujuh where guarantee is the basis.

Core Idea
Profit follows ownership in purchased goods, unless the basis is guarantee (daman) as in mudaraba and wujuh.

In Hanafi commercial law, the division of profit in partnerships is not arbitrary. This lesson unpacks a foundational rule: profit must be proportionate to ownership shares in the goods purchased by the partnership. However, this rule has two important exceptions—mudaraba (capital-labor partnership) and shirkat al-wujuh (credit-based partnership)—where profit is earned through guarantee (daman) rather than direct ownership. Understanding this principle is key to correctly structuring profit-sharing agreements in Islamic finance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default profit rule
The passage states that if partners stipulate equal or proportional ownership in the purchased goods, profit must follow that proportion. For example, if goods are owned half-and-half, profit is half-and-half; if thirds, profit is thirds. This is the general rule for 'inan (limited) partnerships.
2
Understand the three bases for earning profit
Profit is earned through one of three means: capital (mal), labor ('amal), or guarantee (daman). The owner of capital earns by capital, the mudarib (silent partner) earns by labor, and a teacher who teaches for a share earns by guarantee (of knowledge).
The last example shows that guarantee can be a valid basis even outside standard contracts.
3
Apply the rule to an invalid stipulation
If someone says 'dispose of your property on condition that I have its profit' without providing capital, labor, or guarantee, the stipulation is invalid. This reinforces that profit must have a legitimate basis.
4
Recognize the exceptions
In mudaraba and shirkat al-wujuh, profit can be split independently of ownership shares because the basis is guarantee (not capital). In mudaraba, the mudarib guarantees his labor; in wujuh, partners guarantee the credit. Thus, profit shares need not match capital contributions.
The passage clarifies that these exceptions are not analogous to 'inan, where guarantee is absent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(فإن شرطا مناصفة المشترى [قوله : فإن شرطا... الخ ؛ قال في «الهداية ) : فإن شرطا أن المشترى بينهما نصفان، والربح كذلك يجوز ، ولا يجوز أن يتفاضلا فيه، وإن شرطا أن يكون المشترى بينهما أثلاثاً فالربح كذلك ؛ وهذا لأن الربح لا يُستحق إلا بالمال أو العمل أو بالضمان، فرب المال يستحقه بالمال، والمضارب يستحقه بالعمل. والأستاذ الذي يلقي العلم على التلميذ بالنصف بالضمان، ولا يستحق بما سواها، ألا ترى أن من قال لغيره : تصرف في مالك على أنَّ لي ربحه لم يجز ؛ لعدم هذه المعاني واستحقاق الربح في شركة الوجوه بالضمان على ما بيناه. فلا والضمان على قدر الملك في المشترى ، وكانَ الرِّبحُ الزائد عليه ربح ما لم يضمن ، يصح اشتراطه إلا في المضاربة والوجوه، وليست في معناها بخلاف العنان؛ لأنه في
And its absolute form is 'inan (limited partnership), and each is agent for the other in purchasing. If they stipulate equal share of the purchased goods (and its absolute is 'inan, each is agent for the other in purchasing): that is, when the partnership contract is absolute. But if mufawada is stipulated, then each is agent and guarantor for the other. (If they stipulate equal share of the purchased goods) [Comment: He said: If they stipulate that the purchased goods are half and half between them, and likewise the profit, it is permissible. It is not permissible for them to differ in that. If they stipulate that the purchased goods are thirds, then the profit is likewise. This is because profit is not earned except by capital, labor, or guarantee. The owner of capital earns it by capital, the mudarib earns it by labor. Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage discusses how profit is divided in partnerships when the terms are stipulated. It begins by stating that an absolute (mutlaq) partnership is 'inan (limited), where each partner is only an agent for the other, not a guarantor. If they stipulate mufawada, then each is both agent and guarantor. The core ruling is that if the partners stipulate an equal share in the purchased goods (e.g., half each), then profit must also be equal. Similarly, if shares are thirds, profit must be in thirds. The reasoning is that profit is only earned through one of three means: capital, labor, or guarantee. The owner of capital earns profit by his capital, the mudarib by his labor, and the teacher who teaches for a share of profit earns by his guarantee (of knowledge or work). The passage gives an example: if someone says to another 'dispose of your property on condition that I have its profit,' it is invalid because there is no capital, labor, or guarantee from that person. In shirkat al-wujuh, profit is earned by guarantee, and the guarantee is proportional to the ownership share in the purchased goods. Therefore, any excess profit over that proportional share would be profit from what was not guaranteed, and such a stipulation is invalid except in mudaraba and wujuh partnerships (where guarantee is the basis). This is different from 'inan partnership, where the reasoning is distinct.

Key Terms
عنان ('inan): A limited partnership where each partner is an agent for the other in purchasing, and profit/loss is according to capital contribution; no mutual guarantee.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee; in this context, the liability assumed that entitles one to profit (e.g., the mudarib guarantees his labor, the wujuh partner guarantees credit).
مضاربة (mudaraba): A partnership where one provides capital (rabb al-mal) and the other provides labor (mudarib); profits shared as stipulated, losses borne by capital provider.

Key Takeaway
Profit must have a legitimate basis—capital, labor, or guarantee—and in ordinary partnerships, profit follows ownership of the partnership assets.

Shirkah — Partnership

Profit Proportionality in Shirkat al-Wujuh and al-Taqabbul
Explains default type and profit rules for credit/contract partnerships

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that profit in Shirkat al-Wujuh and al-Taqabbul must be proportional to ownership and explain the underlying reasoning based on liability (daman).

Core Idea
Profit in credit partnerships follows ownership because liability (daman) is the basis for entitlement.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies a key rule in Hanafi partnership law: in Shirkat al-Wujuh (credit partnership) and Shirkat al-Taqabbul (contract partnership), the profit share must exactly match the ownership share of the purchased goods. This rule flows from the principle that profit is earned through one of three means: capital, labor, or liability. Here, liability is the operative cause, and it is tied to ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the partnership types
Recognize that Shirkat al-Wujuh and Shirkat al-Taqabbul are partnerships based on creditworthiness and work, not on capital contribution. In their absolute form, they are considered 'inan unless mufawadah is stipulated.
The default partnership type is 'inan, where each partner is an agent. Mufawadah requires additional conditions including equal liability.
2
State the ruling on profit stipulation
From the Hidayah quote: if partners stipulate equal ownership, profit must be equal; if they stipulate unequal ownership (e.g., thirds), profit follows that division. Any stipulation of extra profit beyond ownership share is batil (invalid).
The ruling is conditional on the partnership being of credit type. The same does not apply to 'inan with cash capital.
3
Locate the proof (ta'lil)
The proof is explicitly given: 'profit is earned only through capital, labor, or liability.' In these partnerships, the relevant means is liability (daman) for the purchased goods. Liability is proportional to ownership, so profit must be proportional.
This is a ta'lil (reasoning) based on the nature of the contract, not a direct textual proof from Quran or Sunnah.
4
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given two partners who together buy goods on credit with equal shares but agree that one takes 70% profit: this is invalid because the extra 20% would be profit from goods not guaranteed by that partner.
The exception is mudarabah and Shirkat al-Wujuh themselves, where liability is the basis; 'inan with unspecified capital is different.
5
Distinguish from 'inan partnership
In 'inan where capital is specified (e.g., cash), profit is considered an increase of capital, so ownership determines profit naturally. If capital is unspecified, profit follows agreement more flexibly.
This distinction shows that the rule is specific to credit partnerships, not all partnerships.

Book of Partnership: Its Types and Rulings. And its absolute form is 'inan, and each is the agent of the other in purchase. If they stipulate equal sharing of the purchased item (and its absolute form is 'inan, and each is agent of the other in purchase): meaning when the partnership contract is absolute. But if mufawadah is stipulated in it, then each is agent and guarantor. [If they stipulate equal sharing of the purchased item: The conditions of mufawadah in acceptance are that two craftsmen jointly accept work, guarantee the work equally, are equal in profit and loss, and each is guarantor for the other regarding what befalls him due to the partnership. The form of mufawadah in shirkat al-wujuh: that the two men are eligible for suretyship, the price of the purchase is half between them, they utter the word mufawadah, and are equal in profit. Thus in Al-Muhit, Al-Nihayah, and Al-Fath. [1] His saying: its absolute form; i.e., absolute shirkat al-wujuh, and likewise shirkat al-taqabbul, by not uttering the word mufawadah nor its equivalent, and not stipulating equality in matters that require equality in mufawadah. (2) (2) His saying: and each; i.e., each of the two partners in shirkat al-wujuh and al-taqabbul is agent for the other because the partnership absolutely is not complete without that, as passed. [3] His saying: if they stipulate... etc. He said in Al-Hidayah: if they stipulate that the purchased item is half between them, and the profit likewise, it is permissible; they may not differ in it. If they stipulate that the purchased item is in thirds between them, the profit likewise. This is because profit is not earned except through capital, labor, or liability. The owner of capital earns through capital, the mudarib earns through labor, and the teacher who imparts knowledge to the student earns half through liability, and does not earn by other means. Do you not see that if someone says to another: 'Dispose of your capital on condition that I have its profit,' it is not permissible due to the absence of these meanings. And the earning of profit in shirkat al-wujuh is through liability as we have explained. Liability is commensurate with ownership in the purchased item, and the excess profit is profit of what he did not guarantee, so it is not valid to stipulate it except in mudarabah and shirkat al-wujuh, which are not in its meaning, unlike 'inan; because in it... Or in thirds, the profit likewise, and the stipulation of excess is invalid (or in thirds, the profit likewise, and the stipulation of excess is invalid): meaning if they stipulate that the purchased item is half or thirds between them, and the profit of one exceeds his ownership share, then that stipulation is invalid because profit is commensurate with ownership, lest it lead to profit from what was not guaranteed. Unlike 'inan when the capital is not commodities, for then the capital is not determined by specification, so profit is not an increase of capital as passed. Its meaning in that each of them works in the other's property, so it attaches to it. [1] His saying: or thirds of it; i.e., the purchased item being in thirds between them. [2] His saying: likewise; i.e., on the basis of equal sharing in the case of equal sharing, and on the basis of division into thirds in the case of division into thirds. [3] His saying: and the stipulation of excess; i.e., stipulating extra profit.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a detailed commentary on the rules of partnership, specifically addressing Shirkat al-Wujuh (credit partnership) and Shirkat al-Taqabbul (contract partnership). It begins by establishing the default type of partnership: if the contract is absolute (not specifying mufawadah), it is considered 'inan (a general partnership), where each partner acts as an agent for the other in purchases. If they stipulate mufawadah (equal partnership), then each is both agent and guarantor. The text then outlines the conditions for mufawadah in Shirkat al-Taqabbul: two craftsmen must jointly accept work, guarantee the work equally, share profit and loss equally, and each guarantees the other regarding partnership liabilities. The form of mufawadah in Shirkat al-Wujuh is also described: both must be eligible for suretyship, the purchase price is split evenly, they must verbally express mufawadah, and profit must be equal. The core ruling comes from Al-Hidayah: if partners stipulate equal ownership of purchased goods, profit must be equal; if they stipulate unequal ownership (e.g., thirds), profit follows that division. Any stipulation of profit exceeding the ownership share is invalid (batil). The reasoning (ta'lil) is that profit in such partnerships is earned through liability (daman), not capital or labor. Since liability is tied to ownership of the purchased item, profit must correspond to that ownership. An exception is made for mudarabah and Shirkat al-Wujuh, where profit can be stipulated differently due to their unique nature. The commentary also distinguishes Shirkat al-'Inan: when capital is not specified (like cash), profit is considered an increase of capital, so ownership determines profit. This passage thus clarifies the binding relationship between ownership, liability, and profit in Hanafi partnership law.

Key Terms
شركة الوجوه (shirkat al-wujūh): A partnership based on creditworthiness, where partners buy on credit and sell for profit, without contributing capital.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability or guarantee for the price of purchased goods; the basis for earning profit in Shirkat al-Wujuh.
شركة المفاوضة (shirkat al-mufāwaḍah): An equal partnership where partners are agents and guarantors for each other, with equal shares in profit and loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Shirkat al-Wujuh, if two partners stipulate that the purchased goods are owned equally but one partner takes three-quarters of the profit, what is the ruling on that condition?
A) The condition is valid because they agreed to it.
B) The condition is batil because profit must be proportional to ownership.
C) The condition is makruh but the contract is valid.
D) The condition is valid only if both are from ahl al-kafalah.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, profit in Shirkat al-Wujuh and al-Taqabbul must be exactly proportional to ownership share because liability (daman) is the sole basis of entitlement in these partnerships.

Shirkah — Partnership

Invalidity of Stipulating Excess Profit in Partnership
Ruling on invalid condition of excess profit in partnership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a condition of excess profit in partnership and explain using the principle of al-ghurm bi al-ghunm why it is invalid.

Core Idea
Profit must follow ownership; stipulating excess profit is void because it leads to profiting without liability.

This passage addresses a common pitfall in partnership agreements: partners sometimes agree that one will receive more profit than his ownership share. The Hanafi school rules that such a condition is void. The commentator explains the rationale, linking it to the fundamental rule that profit is based on liability (al-ghurm bi al-ghunm). Understanding this principle prevents unjust enrichment in business contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the condition
Partners stipulate that ownership is equal (e.g., 50-50) but profit distribution is disproportionate (e.g., 70-30).
This is a stipulation of excess profit (shart al-fadl).
2
State the ruling
The condition is batil (void).
The partnership itself remains valid if the condition is removed.
3
Understand the reason
Profit must be proportional to ownership (al-ribh bi qadr al-milk), otherwise it leads to profiting from what one does not guarantee (ribh ma lam yadman).
This applies to partnerships where both partners contribute capital and goods are bought with that capital.
4
Contrast with a special case
In an 'inan partnership where capital is not specified in kind (e.g., not goods), profit may be based on work rather than capital.
This is an exception because the capital is not directly identifiable as the source of profit.

Source Text

Or one-third, so the profit is likewise, and the condition of excess is invalid. Explanation: If they stipulate that the purchased item is half-half or one-third each, and the profit of one exceeds his share, that condition is invalid because profit is proportional to ownership, to avoid profiting from what one is not liable for, unlike an 'inan partnership when the capital is not goods, for then the capital is not determined by specification, so profit is not the growth of capital as previously mentioned in the sense that each works in the other's property, so it joins to it.

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof from the chapter on partnerships. The matn states that if partners stipulate that profit distribution be disproportionate to ownership shares, that condition is invalid. The commentary explains that profit follows ownership; stipulating excess profit would allow a partner to profit from capital he did not guarantee, which is prohibited. This is contrasted with an 'inan partnership where capital is not specified in kind, so profit is considered a reward for work rather than growth of capital.

Key Terms
شرط الفضل (shart al-fadl): A condition stipulating that one partner gets profit exceeding his ownership share.
ربح ما لم يضمن (ribh ma lam yadman): Profit from something one has not guaranteed or is not liable for.
عنان (inan): A type of partnership where each partner contributes capital and works.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Profit must be equal.
D) Profit must be predetermined.

Key Takeaway
Stipulating excess profit beyond ownership is void in Hanafi partnership law because profit is inherently linked to liability.

Invalidity of Partnership in Mubahat (Ownerless Goods)
Ruling that partnership in collecting mubah is invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why partnership in mubahat is invalid in the Hanafi school and state the rule for ownership and compensation when two parties work together.

Core Idea
Partnership in ownerless goods is invalid because agency in mubah is impossible; ownership arises by possession, not contract.

Can two people agree to form a partnership to collect firewood or hunt and share whatever they gather? The Hanafi school says no. The reason goes to the heart of what a partnership contract entails: mutual agency. Since the items are ownerless, neither partner can legally authorize the other to take, because he himself has no ownership or right over the future item. This lesson examines the ruling and its rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? Here, partnership (sharikah) in mubahat (ownerless items) is declared invalid (fasid).
The ruling is not that the items are haram, but that the contract is legally defective.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The text explains that partnership implies agency (wakalah). The proof that the contract is invalid is that agency in taking mubah is itself invalid.
The proof is rational (qiyas), not textual.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal link)
Why is agency in mubah invalid? Because agency requires the principal to command something he can validly do himself. The principal cannot command taking mubah exclusively, as he has no prior right over it.
The agent can own mubah by taking without the principal's command, so the agency is superfluous.
4
Identify the practical consequence
The text clarifies: each partner keeps what he personally acquires. If one assists the other, the worker gets the item and the assistant gets a standard wage (ujr al-mithl).
This ensures no unjust enrichment.
5
Note the scope of the rule
The examples include collecting firewood, grass, hunting, gathering fruits, and even baking bricks from ownerless clay. All fall under mubah.
The list indicates that this ruling covers all forms of ownerless goods.

Source Text

Chapter on Invalid Partnership. (Partnership is not permissible in cutting firewood, gathering grass, and hunting. Whatever each obtains is his.) [1] His saying: 'nor is it permissible...' The principle is that it is not permissible to share in taking something mubah; because the partnership implies agency, and agency in taking mubah is invalid; because it requires the validity of the principal's command, and his command is invalid because it encounters something outside his jurisdiction. Also, the agent owns it by taking without his command, for mubah belongs to the one who gets possession first; hence they said regarding buying sugar or dirhams in a marriage assembly: whoever takes something from it, it is his, and it is not permissible for another to take from him, and one who owns something without the command of another cannot be his deputy in it. As in al-'Inayah and others. [2] His saying: 'cutting firewood' means collecting firewood from deserts, 'gathering grass' means collecting grass, 'hunting', and likewise gathering fruits from mountains, seeking minerals, buried treasure, baking bricks from mubah clay, transporting clay and selling it from mubah land, or pebbles, salt, snow, and similar. As in al-Fath, al-Bazzaziyyah, and others. (3) His saying: 'whatever each obtains...' Its detail as in al-Hidayah and its glosses: In the forms of invalid partnership (which is partnership in mubahat), whatever each obtains without the assistance of the other is his; because ownership in mubahat is established by taking and acquisition. And if one works and the other assists him, such as one uprooting and the other collecting, or one uprooting and collecting and the other carrying, then for the assistant is the wage of the like, and that thing belongs to the worker.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage begins a chapter on invalid partnership. The ruling: partnership in collecting ownerless items (mubah) is not permissible. The reason is that partnership implies mutual agency, but agency in taking mubah is invalid because the principal cannot authorize someone to take something he himself cannot claim exclusively. Moreover, ownership of mubah is established by possession, so the first taker owns it; no one can deputize another for that. The commentary defines the terms and gives detailed rules: if one assists another, the worker gets the item and the assistant gets wages.

Key Terms
الشركة الفاسدة (al-sharikah al-fasidah): An invalid partnership, here specifically partnership in mubahat (ownerless items).
المباح (al-mubah): Something permissible and ownerless that can be acquired by taking possession.
أجر المثل (ujr al-mithl): The standard wage for similar work; compensation for an assistant who helps without ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Partnership in ownerless goods is invalid because the contract cannot create shared ownership where the items are not yet owned; only possession establishes ownership.

The Invalidity of Partnership in Mubah Items
Ruling and distribution rules for invalid partnership in ownerless goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why partnership (shirkah) in taking mubah (ownerless) items is invalid in the Hanafi school and state the default ownership rule when each party acquires independently.

Core Idea
Partnership in mubah is invalid because agency cannot apply to ownerless property; each taker owns what he personally acquires.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on an attempted partnership to collect ownerless goods like firewood or grass. The school holds such a partnership invalid because it would require agency over something no one owns. Understanding this principle clarifies that each participant's rights depend on their own physical taking, not on any agreement. We will explore the rationale and the basic rule for individual acquisition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on partnership (shirkah) in collecting mubah items. Determine what exactly is being ruled upon and whether the hukm is validity or invalidity.
The ruling is that such partnership is not permissible (la yajuz).
2
Understand the rationale: agency in mubah
The author explains that partnership implies agency (wakalah). But agency in taking mubah is invalid because the principal has no authority over mubah and the agent's taking is independent.
The argument is rational (dalil aqli): the agent owns by his own seizure, not by the principal's command.
3
Apply the rule to individual acquisition
If each person acquires without the other's help, each owns what he personally takes. Ownership in mubah is established by physical taking (ihraz).
This default rule applies regardless of any prior agreement.
4
Note the scope: what counts as mubah
The list includes firewood, grass, fish, fruits from mountains, minerals, treasures, clay, salt, snow, etc. The principle applies to any ownerless item.
The list is illustrative, not exhaustive.

فله، وللآخر أجر مثله بالغاً ما بلغ عند محمد. وإن أخذاه معاً فإن علم ما لكل منهما فذاك، وإلا فهو بينهما على التناصف. فإن خلطاه وباعاه قسم الثمن على كيل أو وزن ما لكل منهما، وإن لم يكن وزنياً ولا كيلياً قسم على قيمة ما لكل منها. وإن لم يعلم ما لكل منها صدق كل واحد منهما إلى النصف، لأنهما استويا في الاكتساب، وكان المكتسب في أيديهما، فالظاهر أنه بينهما نصفان، والظاهر يشهد له في ذلك فيقبل قوله، ولا يصدق في الزيادة على النصف، إلا ببينة؛ لأنه يدعي خلاف الظاهر. وذكر في الفتاوى الخيرية أنه لو اجتمع زوج وامرأته وابنه في دار واحدة، وأخذ كل منهما يكتسب على حدة، ويجمعان كسبهما، ولا يعلم التساوي ولا التفاوت ولا التمييز، فيجعل المال بينهما على السوية. وكذلك لو اجتمع أخوة يعملون في تركة أبيهم، ونماء المال فهو بينهم على السوية، وإن اختلفوا في العمل والرأي. وهذا كله في غير الأب وابنه. فقد ذكر في القنية: الأب والابن يكتسبان في صنعة واحدة ولم يكن لهما شيء، فالكسب كله للأب إن كان الابن في عياله؛ لكونه معيناً له. بإعانة الآخر؛ سواء كانت بعمل كالحمل والربط والجمع، أو بآلة كما إذا دفع إليه شبكة ليصيد بها. قال في العناية: تقديمهم قول محمد يؤذن باختياره، وفي المفتاح: إن قول محمد هو المختار للفتوى.
A chapter on invalid partnership. It is not permissible to have a partnership in collecting firewood, grass, and hunting. Whatever each obtains is his own. The principle in this is that partnership is not allowed in taking anything that is mubah (ownerless), because partnership implies agency and being appointed as an agent in taking mubah is invalid, since it requires the validity of the principal's command, but his command is not valid because it encounters a place not within his authority. Also, the agent owns it by his taking without the principal's command, for mubah belongs to the one whose hand reaches it first. Hence they said regarding the purchase of sugar or dirhams in a marriage session: whoever takes something from it, it is his; no one else may take it from him. One who owns something without the command of another is not fit to be his deputy in it. 'Collecting firewood' means gathering firewood from deserts; 'collecting grass' means gathering grass; and hunting, and likewise plucking fruits from mountains, seeking a mine, a pre-Islamic treasure, baking bricks from mubah clay, transporting clay and selling it from mubah land, or pebbles, salt, snow, and the like. Whatever each obtains without the assistance of the other belongs to him, because ownership is established in mubah by taking and appropriating. If one works and the other assists him, such as one uprooting and the other gathering, or one uprooting and gathering and the other carrying, then the assistant has wages similar to his work, and that thing belongs to the worker. What they both take together belongs to them in halves. What he obtains with the assistance of the other belongs to him, and the other has wages similar to his work, whatever it may be, according to Muhammad. If they both take it together, then if what each has is known, that is it; otherwise it is between them in halves. If they mix it and sell it, the price is divided according to the measure or weight of what each has; if it is not a weighty or measureable thing, it is divided according to the value of what each has. If what each has is not known, each is believed up to half, because they are equal in acquisition and the acquired is in their hands, so the apparent presumption is that it is between them in halves, and the apparent presumption supports him in that; so his statement is accepted, but he is not believed regarding more than half except with evidence, because he claims contrary to the apparent presumption. It is mentioned in al-Fatawa al-Khayriyyah that if a husband, his wife, and his son gather in one house, and each of them earns separately, and they combine their earnings, and equality, difference, or distinction is not known, then the money is distributed equally among them. Similarly, if brothers work together in their father's estate and the growth of the money, it is equally among them, even if they differ in work and opinion. All this does not apply to a father and his son. It is mentioned in al-Qunyah: The father and son earn in one craft and they have nothing, then all the earnings belong to the father if the son is under his maintenance, because the son is assisting him. 'With the assistance of the other' whether it is by work like carrying, tying, and gathering, or by an instrument, like if one gives him a net to hunt with. In al-Inayah: Their giving precedence to the statement of Muhammad indicates their choice of it, and in al-Miftah: The statement of Muhammad is the chosen one for fatwa.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding invalid partnership (shirkah fasidah) in collecting mubah (ownerless) items. The commentator explains the rationale: such partnership is invalid because it implies agency (wakalah), but agency in taking mubah is not valid since the principal has no authority over mubah—anyone can take it. Moreover, the agent would own the item by his own seizure, not by the principal's command, so he cannot act as a deputy. The ruling distinguishes between three scenarios: (1) If each acquires without the other's help, each owns his own acquisition. (2) If one works and the other merely assists (e.g., one uproots, the other gathers), the item belongs to the worker, and the assistant is entitled to fair wages (ujr al-mithl). This is the position of Imam Muhammad, which is the chosen view for fatwa. (3) If both acquire together, the item is shared equally. If they mix the items and sell them, the price is divided proportionally by weight, measure, or value. If the exact contributions are unknown, each is believed up to half. The passage also applies these principles to joint earnings in a household: husband, wife, and son earning separately but pooling income are treated equally unless the father and son case where the son is dependent. This commentary underscores the Hanafi principle that ownership in mubah is based on actual physical taking (ihraz), not on contractual arrangement.

Key Terms
شركة فاسدة (shirkah fasidah): A partnership that is legally defective; here specifically a partnership in taking mubah items, which is invalid because it implies agency in something not subject to agency.
مباح (mubah): Ownerless property that anyone may acquire, e.g., firewood, grass, fish, etc.
أجر المثل (ujr al-mithl): Fair market wages for similar work done by an assistant in a partnership context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is partnership in collecting firewood (mubah) invalid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because firewood is not a permissible object of trade.
B) Because it implies agency in mubah, which is not valid since the principal has no authority over mubah.
C) Because partnership requires a written contract.
D) Because firewood is a necessity and cannot be owned.

Key Takeaway
A partnership to collect ownerless goods is invalid in Hanafi fiqh because agency cannot operate over mubah; each person owns only what he physically takes.

Shirkah — Partnership

Distribution Rules in Invalid Mubah Partnerships
Ruling and distribution rules for invalid partnership in ownerless goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distribution rules for invalid mubah partnerships: distinguish between assistance (ujr al-mithl) and joint taking (equal shares), and handle cases of uncertainty and household earnings.

Core Idea
In an invalid mubah partnership, the worker owns the item and the assistant gets wages; if both take jointly, they share equally.

This lesson builds on the invalidity of partnership in mubah by detailing how to distribute the collected items. The text provides rules for three scenarios: when one assists the other, when both take together, and when contributions are unknown. It also extends these principles to family members earning in common. Understanding these rules prevents disputes and ensures just compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relationship: worker vs assistant
Determine who actually performed the physical taking (worker) and who merely helped (assistant).
The worker is the one who uproots, cuts, or collects; the assistant carries, ties, or provides tools.
2
Apply the rule for assistance
Imam Muhammad's view (mufta bihi): the item belongs to the worker, and the assistant gets fair wages (ujr al-mithl). This applies even if the assistant's help is via an instrument (e.g., providing a net).
The text says 'whether by work or by instrument'.
3
Apply the rule for joint taking
If both physically take the item together (e.g., both grab the same fish), they are co-owners equally (nisfayn). If they mix their separate acquisitions and sell, proceeds are divided by weight/measure or value.
When contributions are unknown, each is believed up to half because of the apparent presumption (zahir) of equality.
4
Apply to household joint earnings
For a husband, wife, and son earning separately but pooling income, it is distributed equally. For a father and son where the son is dependent, all earnings go to the father.
This is an exception based on maintenance and dependency.

Key Terms
أجر المثل (ujr al-mithl): Fair market wages for similar work done by an assistant in a partnership context.
تناصف (tanasuf): Dividing into halves; equality in shares when joint taking or when contributions are unknown.
ظاهر (zahir): Apparent presumption; here, the presumption that each party contributed equally when the actual amounts are unknown.

Joint Acquisition and Assistance in Partnership – Division of Earnings
Explaining division of earnings when two acquire together or one assists

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between joint acquisition and assistance in a Hanafi partnership, and state the appropriate division of earnings, including the rule of ajr al-mithl.

Core Idea
Equal division for joint taking; compensation not ownership for the assistant.

In partnership or joint work, the division of earnings depends on the nature of participation. The Hanafi school provides clear rules: when two persons physically acquire something together, it is split equally. But if one person acquires with the other's assistance, the assistant is entitled to a fair wage (ajr al-mithl), not a share of the item. This lesson unpacks the ruling with examples and exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the first sentence of the Arabic passage. What are the two situations described? One is joint acquisition (ما أخذاه معاً), the other is acquisition with assistance (ما حصل له بإعانة الآخر).
The ruling for each is different.
2
Analyze the rationale for equal division
The commentator says 'because it is the apparent case when they jointly acquire.' This means when both physically take, it is presumed equal unless proven otherwise. Think about why this presumption is practical in partnerships.
The principle is based on apparent circumstances (الظاهر).
3
Understand the concept of ajr al-mithl
When one assists, he does not own the item; he gets a standard wage (ajr al-mithl) for his help. The passage emphasizes 'regardless of how much' according to Imam Muhammad, indicating no cap. Compare this with Abu Yusuf's view (covered later).
Assistance can be physical or by providing a tool.
4
Apply the rule to the family examples
The passage gives examples: husband-wife-son working together; brothers in a father's estate. They get equal shares if earnings are mixed. But a father and dependent son: all earnings go to the father because the son is considered an assistant.
The father-son exception highlights the principle of dependency.
5
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad says the assistant gets full ajr al-mithl without limit; Abu Yusuf caps it at half the value. The later scholars (as per al-'Inayah) preferred Muhammad's view. Identify which view is mufta bihi.
This difference affects practical compensation amounts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما أخذاه معاً فلهما نصفين، وما حصل له بإعانة الآخر فله، وللآخر أجر مثلِهِ بالغاً ما بلغ عند محمد
[١] قوله : نصفين؛ لكونه الظاهـر حـين تحصيلهما معاً ، وعلى هذا ذكر في الفتاوى الخيرية» (٢) أنه لو اجتمع زوج وامرأته وابنه في دار واحدة، وأخذ كل منهما يكتسب على حدة، ويجمعان كسبهما ، ولا يعلمُ التّساوي ولا التفاوت ولا التمييز، فيجعل المالُ بينهما على السوية، وكذلك لو اجتمع أخوة يعملون في تركة أبيهم، ونماء المال فهو بينهم على السوية، وإن اختلفوا في العمل والرأي وهذا كله في غير الأب وابنه. فقد ذكر في «القنية» (۳) : الأب والابن يكتسبان في صنعة واحدة ولم يكن لهما شيء، فالكسب كله للأب إن كان الابن في عياله؛ لكونه معيناً له. [٢) قوله : بإعانة الآخر؛ سواء كانت بعمل كالحمل والربط والجمع، أو بآلة كما إذا دفع إليه شبكة ؛ ليصيد بها. [٣]قوله : عند محمد ه ; قال في «العناية»(4) : تقديمهم قول محمد الله وكذا تقديم
And what they both take together is for them in halves, and what he obtains with the assistance of the other is for him, and for the other is the like wage (ajr mithlihi) regardless of how much, according to Muhammad. [1] He said: 'halves'; because it is the apparent (case) at the time of their jointly acquiring. And upon this it is mentioned in al-Fatawa al-Khayriyyah: that if a husband, his wife, and his son reside in one house, and each of them earns separately and they combine their earnings, and neither equality nor disparity nor distinction is known, then the wealth is made equal between them. Similarly, if brothers work in their father's estate and the increase of wealth, it is between them equally, even if they differ in work and opinion. And all this is other than the father and his son. It is mentioned in al-Qunyah: the father and son earn in one craft and they have nothing, then the entire earning is for the father if the son is under his support, because he is an assistant to him. [2] He said: 'with the assistance of the other'; whether it is by work such as carrying, tying and collecting, or by tool such as if he hands him a net to catch with it. [3] He said: 'according to Muhammad'; he said in al-'Inayah: they gave precedence to the statement of Muhammad, and also gave precedence...
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage deals with the distribution of earnings when two persons work together in a partnership or similar arrangement. The base ruling states that if they both physically take something together, it is divided equally between them. However, if one person obtains something with the assistance of the other—for example, one picks up an item while the other helps by holding or collecting—then the item belongs to the one who primarily took it, and the assistant is entitled to a fair wage (ajr al-mithl) for his labor, according to Imam Muhammad. The commentator expands with examples: if a family lives together and earns jointly without clear distinction, the earnings are split equally. This also applies to brothers working in their father's estate. An exception is when a father and son work together and the son is under the father's maintenance—then all earnings go to the father because the son is considered an assistant. The commentator clarifies that assistance can be through physical work or by providing a tool like a net.

Key Terms
أجر المثل (ajr al-mithl): The standard wage or compensation for similar work in the market.
معاونة (mu'aawanah): Assistance that is not the primary cause of acquisition; includes physical help or providing tools.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, if two persons work together and one obtains an item with the other's assistance, what is the assistant entitled to?
A) Half of the item.
B) A like wage (ajr al-mithl) without limit.
C) A like wage not exceeding half the price.
D) Nothing, because he is just an assistant.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi partnership earnings, joint taking yields equal shares; assistance yields fair compensation, not co-ownership.

Dispute on Limit of Compensation and Rulings on Irrigation and Corrupt Partnership
Resolving an intra-Hanafi dispute on compensation cap and stating related partnership rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute on the cap for ajr al-mithl and apply the rulings on irrigation partnership and profit in fasid partnerships.

Core Idea
An assistant's wage cap is disputed: Abu Yusuf halves it; Muhammad says unlimited, and later Hanafis preferred Muhammad.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya resolves an intra-Hanafi dispute over how much an assistant partner should be paid when the main worker acquires an item. It also clarifies two related rulings: why partnership in irrigation is invalid and how profit is distributed in a defective partnership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed cap
Recognize that Abu Yusuf limits ajr al-mithl to half the item's price, while Imam Muhammad allows full compensation.
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's reasoning
Abu Yusuf argues the assistant consented to half the total, so his wage should not exceed half.
3
Recognize the preferred view
Later scholars preferred Muhammad's position (unlimited ajr al-mithl), as cited in al-'Inayah and al-Mabsut.
Tarjih mechanism: later scholars gave weight to Muhammad's view.
4
Apply the irrigation ruling
Partnership in watering is invalid because water is mubah (free property); the worker gets earnings but must pay a fair wage for the use of the other's property.
Key concept: mubah cannot be subject of a partnership contract.
5
Apply the fasid partnership ruling
Profit in an invalid partnership is distributed according to capital contributed, not any stipulated ratio.
Fasid vs batil: fasid contracts still yield some rights.

Source Text

ولا يزادُ على نصف ثمنه عند أبي يوسف . ولا في الاستسقاء بأن كان لأحدهما بغل، وللآخر ،راوية واستقى أحدهما ، فالكسب للعامل، وعليه أجر مثل ما للآخر. والرِّبحُ في الشركة الفاسدة على قدر المال

[۱] قوله : على نصف ثمنه ; أي يعطي للمعين أجر المثل إن كان مثل نصف الثَّمَنِ أو أقل، فلو أكثر لا يزاد على نصف الثَّمن ; لأنه رضي بنصف المجموع، وإن كان مجهولاً في الحال، ثم التعبير بنصف الثَّمن وقع في «الهداية»(۳)، وغيرها. وذكر في «النقاية»(4) وغيرها : إن أجر المثلِ لا يزادُ على نصف القيمة : أي نصف قيمة المباح يوم الأخذ إن كان له قيمة وهذا هو الأولى; لأن المعين يطلب أجر المثل عند تمام العمل، وربما لا يتيسر البيعُ إلى ذلك الوقت، فكيف يفرض نصف ثمنه حتى يطلب.
(٢) قوله : ولا في الاستسقاء; أي لا تجوز الشركة في سقي الماء; لأنَّ الماء من الأمور المباحة فلا تنعقد الشركة لإحرازه. [٣] قوله : راوية; هي في الأصل الجمل التي يحملُ عليها الماء، ثم استعمل في المزادة، وهي الجلود المصنوعة لنقل الماء، كذا في «الفتح»
(٤) قوله : على قدر المال ; لأن الربح إنما يتبع العقد إذا كان صحيحاً ; فإذا فسد
And it is not increased beyond half its price according to Abu Yusuf. And not in irrigation: such that one has a mule, the other has a waterskin, and one draws water, then the earning is for the worker, and upon him is the like wage of what is for the other. And the profit in an invalid partnership is according to the capital.

[1] He said: 'on half its price'; that is, he gives to the assistant the like wage if it is like half the price or less, but if more, it is not increased beyond half the price, because he consented to half the total, even if it was unknown at the time. Then the expression 'half the price' occurs in al-Hidayah and others. And it is mentioned in al-Niqayah and others: the like wage is not increased beyond half the value, i.e., half the value of the mubah (free property) on the day of taking if it has a value, and this is more appropriate, because the assistant demands the like wage at completion of the work, and perhaps the sale is not facilitated until that time, so how can half its price be assumed so that he demands? [2] He said: 'and not in irrigation'; that is, partnership in watering is not valid, because water is among permissible things, so partnership is not contracted for its acquisition. [3] He said: 'rawiyah'; it is originally the camel on which water is carried, then used for the waterskin, which is the leather made for transporting water, as in al-Fath. [4] He said: 'according to the capital'; because profit follows the contract if it is valid; but if it becomes corrupt...
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the compensation (ajr al-mithl) for an assistant partner. According to Imam Muhammad, the compensation is unlimited—whatever fair wage is due. According to Imam Abu Yusuf, the compensation cannot exceed half the market price or value of the acquired item. The reasoning given for Abu Yusuf's view is that the assistant implicitly consented to take half the total when he agreed to help, so he should not claim more than half. However, the commentator notes that later scholars preferred the view of Imam Muhammad, as indicated by the statement in al-Mabsut that they chose his opinion. The passage also covers two other rulings: First, a partnership in irrigation (using a mule and waterskin) is invalid because water is mubah (free property) and partnership cannot be formed to acquire something already permissible. The earnings from such work go to the worker, and he must pay the other partner a fair wage for the use of his property. Second, in an invalid (fasid) partnership, any profit is distributed according to the capital contributed, not according to any stipulated ratio, because profit follows the contract only when it is valid.

Key Terms
أجر المثل (ajr al-mithl): Market-rate compensation for the assistant's work, subject to a cap according to Abu Yusuf.
شركة فاسدة (shirkah fasidah): A partnership that is defective in its contract, e.g., invalid conditions, but still has some effect.
استسقاء (istisqa'): Irrigation or drawing water; partnership for watering is invalid because water is mubah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling regarding partnership in irrigation (istisqa')?
A) It is valid and profits are shared equally.
B) It is invalid because water is mubah.
C) It is valid only if one provides the animal.
D) It is makruh but still enforceable.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an assistant's wage is not capped (Muhammad's view), irrigation partnership is invalid, and fasid partnership profits follow capital.
